===== PAGE 8 =====
Chapter 1
Fundamental concepts
The objective of this section is to realize that the meaning of a statistical model essentially depends on
an identification analysis. This is due to the fact that identifiability is related to model construction,
not to statistical estimation. This explains why some authors consider an identification problem as a
pre-statistical problem (McHugh, 1956).
1.1 The problems of statistics
In 1922, R. A. Fisher pointed out that, in spite of the large amount of fruitful applications of statistics,
its basic principles were still in a “state of obscurity” and that “during the recent rapid development of
practical methods, fundamental problems have been ignored” (Fisher, 1922, p.310). This evaluation led
Fisher to define the scope of statistical methods as the reduction of data. “No human mind is capable
of grasping in its entirety the meaning of any considerable quantity of numerical data” (Fisher, 1973,
p.6). Consequently, data should be replaced by a relatively few quantity of values which shall contain
the relevant information provided by the original data.
Conceptually speaking, the reduction of data is accomplished by constructing a hypothetical infinite
population, of which the actual data are considered a random sample. This hypothetical population is
fully characterized by a probability distribution, which in turn is specified by relatively few parameters.
The parameters “are sufficient to describe it exhaustively in respect of all qualities under discussion”.
Thus, any information provided by a sample, which is used to estimate those parameters, is relevant
information. This conceptual scheme leads to distinguish three problems of statistics (Fisher, 1922,
1973, Chapter 1): Problems of Specification , Problems of Estimation and Problems of Distribution .
Problems of Specification arise in the choice of the mathematical form of the probability distribution
which characterizes the observed population. Its choice “is not arbitrary, but requires an understanding
of the way in which the data are supposed to, or did in fact, originate” (Fisher, 1973, p.8).
How to make precise the way in which the data are generated? A quarter of century later, Koopmans
and Reiersøl (1950) suggested a response, which leads to reformulate the specification problem as de-
fined by Fisher. Such a reformulation, “appropriate to many applications of statistical methods”, is based
1

===== PAGE 9 =====
on a distinction between population and structure:
In many fields the objective of the investigator’s inquisitiveness is not just a “population” in the sense
of a distribution of observable variables, but a physical structure projected behind this distribution,
by which the latter is thought to be generated. The word “physical” is used merely to convey that the
structure concept is based on the investigator’s ideas as to the “explanation” or “formation” of the
phenomena studied, briefly, on his theory of these phenomena, whether they are classified as physical
in the literal sense, biological, psychological, sociological, economic or otherwise (Koopmans &
Reiersøl, 1950, p.165).
A structure is determined on the basis of a substantive theory, combined with systematically collected
data for the relevant variables. The collected data are represented by observed variables, whereas (some)
aspects of a substantive theory are represented by unobservable or latent ones. Thus, a structure is
composed of a particular probability distribution of the latent variables, combined with a relationship
between the observed and unobserved variables. Koopmans and Reiersøl’s reformulation of the specifi-
cation problem is accordingly defined as follows:
The specification is therefore concerned with the mathematical forms of both the distribution of the
latent variables and the relationships connecting observed and latent variables (p.168).
The distribution of the observed variables is regarded as produced by an operation performed on the dis-
tribution of the unobserved variables. The specification problem is, therefore, concerned with specifying
a model which, by hypothesis, contains the structure generating the distribution of the observed variables.
The result of this reformulation is the emergence of the identification problem: can the distribution of
the observations, generated by a given structure, be generated by only one structure in that model? When
the answer is affirmative, it is said that the structure is identified by the observations. In such a case,
the structure has a statistical meaning in the sense that it is supported by the observations through the
probability distribution of the observations (Koopmans, 1949; Hurwicz, 1950; Clifford, 1982; Manski,
1995; San Mart´ın, 2015). The main characteristic of the so-called structural modeling is that it considers
structures and/or parameters as endowed with a meaning that should be supported by the observations.
1.2 Statistical model
The specification problem leads to define a statistical model. Its definition becomes apparent after mak-
ing explicit the components of a statistical model:
• Sample space pS, Sq, where S is the set of all possible outcomes, and S is the σ-field of subsets of
S: the elements of S are considered as the events of interest.
• A probability distribution P θ defined on the sample space pS, Sq, where θ is a parameter. P θ is
accordingly called sampling distribution or sampling probability.
2

===== PAGE 10 =====
• θ P Θ, where the set Θ is called parameter space.
Thus, a statistical model can compactly be written as
E “ tpS, Sq, P θ : θ P Θu.
When the statistical model is dominated (that is, when for all θ P Θ, P θ is absolutely continuous with
respect to a σ-finite measure λ defined on pS, S), the statistical model is equivalently described through
the likelihood function pθ, where pθ “ dP θ
dλ :
E “ tpS, Sq : pθ : θ P Θu.
Remark 1.1 It is important to remark that this concept covers what it is commonly called parametric,
semi-parametric and non-parametric models: the distinction depends on the structure of Θ. Thus, the
parameter space Θ of a parametric model is contained in a finite-dimensional space; for non-parametric
models, the parameter space Θ is an infinite-dimensional space; semi-parametric models can be de-
scribed by saying that Θ is a cartesian product of the formA ˆΨ, where A is a finite-dimensional space
and Ψ is an infinite-dimensional space.

and a random effect model: it is enough to make explicit the data generating process. As an example, let
us consider an ANOV A 1 Fixed Factor (balanced case):

3

1. The specification (1.2.3) does not correspond to the statistical model because a statistical model
describes the observations.
2. (1.2.3.i) is a conditional distribution, whereas (1.2.1) is a marginal distribution: therefore, both
distributions are not related between them and consequently one specification can not be viewed
as a kind of “re-interpretation” of the other (as it is typically viewed in econometrics, statistics,
biometrics or psychometrics). Moreover, in (1.2.1) βj is a constant, whereas in (1.2.3.i) it is a
random variable.
3. c.ind means that the for each j, tYij : i “ 1, . . . , nu are mutually independent conditionally on βj.
The specification (1.2.3) induces a statistical model: it is obtained after integrating out the βj’s. For
each j, it follows that

This means that two observations Yij and Yi1j are correlated between them. Therefore, a random effect
model is used when the scientist have reason to believe that the observations nested in the j-group are
correlated in the sense that the j-group induces such a correlation. This is not the case in a fixed-effect
model. The choice depends on substantive considerations related to the fact that the j-group induces
correlation or not.
■
1.3 Identification problem
The definition of statistical model implicitly involves a relationship between the parameter space and
the set of sampling distributions defined on pS, Sq. If we denote this set as PpS, Sq, such a relationship
corresponds to the mapping Φ : Θ Ý ÑPpS, Sq such that Φpθq “ P θ for θ P Θ.
Our interest is focused on the sample space pS, Sq: there we collect specific features of interest char-
acterizing a particular population. For instance, the number of correct responses in a test of 40 items.
A sampling probability P θ is supposed to describe the behavior of those features. This behavior could
be interpreted as the variability of such features across the population. The relevant point here is that
P θ fully characterizes the population that is analyzed with respect to those features of interest. If we
accept that, then we focus our interest on the family tP θ : θ P Θu, assuming that θ is a characteristic of
P θ which in turn describes the units of the population in terms of those features of interest. As Fisher
said, the parameters “are sufficient to describe [the population of interest] exhaustively in respect of all
qualities under discussion” (Fisher, 1973).
Paraphrasing Koopmans (1949), statistical inference unsupported by a substantive theory applies to
whatever statistical regularities and, consequently, stable relationships can be discerned in the data. Such
4

===== PAGE 12 =====
purely empirical relationships are likely to be due to the presence and persistence of underlying structural
relationships. However, the direction of this deduction cannot be reversed (from the empirical to the
structural relationships) expect possibly with the help of a substantive theory which specifies the form of
the structural relationships. The possibility of this deduction necessarily implies to solve anidentification
problem at the model construction level. As Haavelmo (1997) puts it,
The one type of problems, seemingly paradoxical, grew out of a rather intricate consequence of
successful economic theory. Strangely enough, the fact is that if an economic theory, an economic
relation, is a good theory, true to reality, it may not be possible to quantify it by using data from the
economic of which that relation is a part. This is the so-called “problem of identification” (p.14).
These considerations are applicable to all social and health sciences, among many others. This is a
requirement of a scientific inquiry that focuses on the explanation of the phenomena, and not (only) on
the mere prediction with implicit assumptions of structural stability (as is the case with most of the recent
data science techniques).
The previous considerations lead to conclude that the properties of a population under study are repre-
sented by a parameter. Consequently, if a property supposed to be a characteristic of a population can not
be represented by a parameter of the sampling probability, such a property is not supported by the obser-
vations: this corresponds to an identification problem. Therefore, the statistical meaning of parameters
becomes explicit by an identification analysis.
Ejemplo 1.3.1 [From Fari ˜na, Gonz´alez, and San Mart´ın (2019)]Let us illustrate these considerations
with the ANOV A 1 Fixed factor as introduced in Example 1.2.1. For this model, one of the following two
identifiability restrictions are typically considered: β1 “ 0 or řJ
j“1 βj “ 0. Under the first restriction
parameter identifiability follows from the identity α “ µ1 “ EpYi1q and βj “ µj ´ µ1 “ EpYijq ´
EpYi1q. Thus, under the first restriction, α corresponds to the mean of the first population, whereas βj
represents the deviation of thejth population mean with respect to the mean of the first population. Under
the second restriction, we have that α “ 1
J
řJ

J

EpYijq ´ 1

corresponds to the deviation of thejth population mean with respect to the overall mean. The situation is
different for the random-effects version of the model (1.2.3): the identifiability follows from the induced
statistical model (1.2.4). Here, α “ Epµjq “ EpYijq, that is, it represents a common mean for each
observation.

person i answers correctly item j is greater than the probability of answering incorrectly. This property
is know as the simultaneous representation “personˆ items”. For an application, see Claro et al. (2012).
■
1.4 Concluding remarks
Identification analysis is a relevant component of model construction: the empirical interpretation of
the parameters of interest depend on this type of analysis. Furthermore, all property that a scientist
assumes to be a characteristic of a population under study is actually a characteristic of it only if is a
function of the corresponding sampling distribution; for more details, see also San Mart ´ın (2018). This
aspect becomes relevant for empirical research because such a researchalways involves an identification
problem. The way in which the identification problem is solved will constitute the so-called maintained
assumption. When this type of assumptions is combined with empirical evidence, we reach scientific
conclusions and thus we conform a corpus of scientific knowledge (Manski, 2013a). Based on this
knowledge, science becomes useful for informing policy decisions. Nevertheless, we will emphasize
that the maintained assumptions are not unique and, therefore, we will make explicit the subjective side
of scientific knowledge. This is one of the aspects we will discuss in what follows.
7

2.1 Introduction
Let us consider the scores that students obtained in the 2013 SIMCE Mathematics test (a Chilean national
test): those students were at 6th grade of elementary school. In order to have a panoramic view of
these data, we will begin by describing some general elements that reflect the way in which the Chilean
Educational System is organized.
‚ Students belong to schools which are classified in three categories: Public schools (denoted by MN),
subsidized schools (denoted bby PS) and private schools (denoted by PP). From the data we have
1. 39.39% of students belong to MN schools;
2. 52.96% of students belong to PS schools;
3. 7.65% of students belong to PP schools.
‚ Schools are also classified in terms of socioeconomic level. The Chilean Agency of Quality of Edu-
cation defines 5 socioeconomic levels, which are constructed from the information collected in surveys
applied to parents and guardians. Five socioeconomic levels are defined, from the lowest to the highest.
From the data we have
1. 10.45% of students belong to schools of low socioeconomic level (denoted by 1).
2. 31.54% of students belong to schools of middle low socioeconomic level (denoted by 2).
3. 34.30% of students belong to schools of middle socioeconomic level (denoted by 3).

moment’s thought:

the distribution function.

This means that a phenomenon of interest, which is described by means of the probability distribution,
can be considered as the marginalization of several conditional distributions. In the example at hand
we can say that the distribution of scores in the SIMCE Mathematics test corresponds to the expected
value of the distributions conditional on the administrative type of school; or to the expected value of the
socioeconomic group of the school; or to the expected value of the biological sex of the students. Each
of these variables, D, S, G, is related to the score. And there may be hundreds. In fact, the Law of Total
Probabilities can be written as follows:
P pX ď xq “ ErP pX ď x | W qs @ x P N,
for all random variables W defined on M.
Conditional probability is used to model relationships between random variables, a matter already
explained by Laplace (see below); therefore, the above equality tells us that a marginal phenomenon can
be seen as the expected value of many conditional phenomena: the data do not provide a criterion for
choosing variables on which to condition . To get a criterion, it is imperative to turn to and collaborate
with experts in the fields from which the data come. The task of statisticians is to model the contextual
knowledge they have. A purely exploratory analysis (as it is done by Data Science, following Gauss and
Tukey) can be spurious whenever it pretends to replace the intuitions of the experts of the phenomena
under study.

P pBq “ ErP pB | As,
where A “ tAq : q “ 1, . . . , ru. The numbers P pB | Aqq can be computed using the computation rule
P pB | Aqq “ P pB X Aqq
P pAqq , si P pAqq ‰ 0.
How can we define the conditional probability P pB | mathcalAq if, for example, P pA1q “ 0? Let use
the characterization of a conditional probability :
P pBq “ ErP pB | Aqs
“ P pB | A1qP pA1q ` P pB | A2qP pA2q ` ¨ ¨ ¨ ` P pB | ArqP pArq.
The quantity on the left side of the equality does not change whatever the value ofP pB | A1q P r 0, 1s is,
since that value multiplied by 0 (which is P pA1q) will always give 0. Therefore, the number P pB | A1q
is defined arbitrarily, without arbitrarily changing the random variable P pB | mathcalAq.

2.7 Marginal-conditional decomposition
The concept of conditional probability is intimately related to the statistical modeling of dependent
events. This is specified through what we call marginal-conditional decomposition. In order to make
such a decomposition explicit, we will use an argument due to Laplace (1820, pp. 181-182):
Suppose that the total number of possible cases that can occur is equal to p. Suppose further that
1. From the p possible cases, there are p1 cases favorable to the occurrence of an event E1.
2. From these p1 cases, there are q cases favorable to the occurrence of event E2.
These conditions imply that if event E1 occurs, not necessarily event E2 occurs. However, if event
E2 occurs, then necessarily event E1 occurs. Therefore, events E1 and E2 are dependent in the sense
that the occurrence of one of them depends on the occurrence of the other, although such dependence is
not symmetric. More precisely, the conditions defined by one of the events determine the probability of
occurrence of the other event.
As an example, let us consider that E1 corresponds to the event “cloudy day in a given year”, whileE2
corresponds to the event “rainy day in a given year”. Thus, if it rains, then it is necessarily cloudy (the
“being cloudy” is determined by the rainy day), but if it is cloudy it does not necessarily rain: raining is
an event contingent to being cloudy.
Now, the probability of the occurrence of event E1 is given by
p1
p .
In the context of the example, this corresponds to the proportion of cloudy days in a given year. The
probability of the occurrence of event E2 under the condition that event E1 occurred is given by
q
p1 .
In the context of the example, this corresponds to the proportion of rainy days among cloudy days –that
is, a relative proportion. Finally, the probability of occurrence simultaneously of events E1 and E2 is
given by q
p .
In the context of the example, this would correspond to the proportion of rainy (and hence cloudy) days
in a given year.
The above three probabilities are related to each other as follows:
q
p “ p1
p ˆ q
p1 ; (2.7.3)
22

===== PAGE 30 =====
that is, the probability of the compound eventE1 X E2 is equal to the probability of eventE1, multiplied
by the probability of event E2, under the assumption that E1 has occurred. In the context of the exam-
ple, this means that the proportion of rainy (and therefore cloudy) days in a given year is equal to the
proportion of cloudy days, multiplied by the proportion of rainy days among cloudy days.
Now, let us denote by P pE1 X E2q, the probability of the compound event E1 X E2; the probability
of event E1 we denote by P pE1q; and the probability of occurrence of event E2, under the condition
determined by event E1, we denote by P pE2 | E1q. Then the relation (2.7.3) is written as
P pE1 X E2q “ P pE1q P pE2 | E1q. (2.7.4)
The decomposition (2.7.4) corresponds to a marginal-conditional decomposition: it indicates to what
extent the conditions defined by event E1 determine the probability of occurrence of event E2.
This decomposition can be extended to describe the joint occurrence of two events E1 and E2 under
the condition determined by an event C:
P pE1 X E2 | Cq “ P pE1 | Cq P pE2 | E1 X Cq. (2.7.5)
The decompositions (2.7.4) and (2.7.5) are thus useful for modeling dependent events.
23

===== PAGE 31 =====
Chapter 3
What can we learn when data are missing?
Typically, missing data are considered as an estimation problem. It is claimed that missing data cause
estimation problem because they lead to use only the observable information. The statistical literature
provides a plethora of methods leading to solve the problem in such a way that a data set with missing
data is “transformed” into a “complete data set” (that is, a data set without missing data). However, this
perspective is incorrect: the missing data is a property inherent to a specific data generating process and,
therefore, it should be considered as a part of the specification problem. In this chapter, we will consider
the missing data problem as a modelling problem, emphasizing the underlying identification problem.
This chapter is based on Manski (2005b).
3.1 Structure of the problem
Let J denotes a population of statistical units. For each j P J, we have an outcome yj in a space Y .
Thus, we have a probability space pJ, Ω, P q and a random variable y : J Ý Ñ Y with a probability
distribution P pyq.
A sampling process draws statistical units at random from J. A realization of y may or may not be
observable as indicated by the realization of a binary random variable z: y is observable if z “ 1, and y
is not observable if z “ 0. Using the available information, is it possible to identify P pyq? If not, what
can we learn about P pyq?
The structure of the missing data problem is displayed by the Law of Total Probability:
P pyq “ P py | z “ 1qP pz “ 1q ` P py | z “ 0qP pz “ 0q. (3.1.1)
The sampling process reveals the distribution of the observable outcomes P py | z “ 1q and the distri-
bution of observability P pzq. In other words, both P py | z “ 1q and P pzq are identified because such
distributions are the parameters itself. However, the sampling process is not informative regarding the
distribution of missing outcomes P py | z “ 0q. Hence the empirical evidence reveals that P pyq lies in
24

===== PAGE 32 =====
the identification region
HrP pyqs .“ tP py | z “ 1qP pz “ 1q ` γ P pz “ 0q, γ P ΓY u, (3.1.2)
where ΓY denotes the space of all probability distributions on Y .
The feasible values of P pyq are the mixtures of P py | z “ 1q and all elements of ΓY , with mixing
probabilities P pz “ 1q and P pz “ 0q. Consequently, if we choose γ1 ‰ γ2, then the induced P pyq’s are
coherent with the empirical evidence. Therefore, P pyq is not identified.
Note that
HrP pyqs Ă ΓY if 0 ă P pz “ 0q ă 1;
and
HrP pyqs “ t P py | z “ 1qu if P pz “ 0q “ 0.
Hence P pyq is partially identifiedwhen 0 ă P pz “ 0q ă 1 and it ispoint-identified when P pz “ 0q “ 0.
Ejemplo 3.1.1 A test of mathematics was intended to be applied to 1020 students. Each student vol-
untarily chooses to answer the test or not. Only 557 students answered the test. The test was scored
between 1 and 7. If a student obtains at least 4, then she or he is considered to master the content of the
test. The available empirical evidence reveals P pzq, namely
P pz “ 1q “ 557
1020 “ 0.55, P pz “ 0q “ 114633
670 “ 0.45;
and the distribution P py | z “ 1q of observable scores, which is depicted in Figure 3.1.
The identification region of P pyq is plotted in Figure 3.2: it can be realized the uncertainty induced by
the missing data, which means that there are a lot of possible probability distributions P pyq.

Distributional assumptions may have identifying power. One may assert that the distribution P py |
z “ 0q lies in some set Γ0Y Ă ΓY . Then the identification region shrinks from HrP pyqs to
H1rP pyqs .“ tP py | z “ 1qP p“ 1q ` γ P pz “ 0q, γ P Γ0Y u. (3.1.3)
Or one may assert that the distribution of interest P pyq lies in some set H0rP pyqs Ă ΓY . Then the
identification region shrinks from HrP pyqs to
H1rP pyqs .“ H0rP pyqs X HrP pyqs.
Assumptions of the former and the latter types differ in that the former are necessarily non-refutable
but the latter may be refutable. As a matter of fact,
1. An assumption that restricts P py | z “ 0q is non-refutable because one does not observe the

3.3 Parameters that respect stochastic dominance
Let us consider the identification region discussed in Example 3.1.1, Figure 3.2. The fact that
P py ď t, z “ 1q ď P py ď tq @ t P r1, 7s,
means that the distribution P py ď t, z “ 1q stochastically dominates P py ď tq. In this case, the mean
of the dominating distribution (namely, 3.1; see Example 3.2.1) is smaller or equal than the mean of the
dominated distribution (namely, 5.8). It is said that the mean is a parameter that respects the stochas-
tic dominance. This motivates the analysis of partial identifiability of parameters respecting stochastic
dominance, which will be discussed in this section.
Let us introduce the following definitions:
Definition 3.1 Let ΓR be the space of probability distributions on the extended real line R. Distribution
F P ΓR stochastically dominates the distribution F 1 P ΓR is
F ptq ď F 1ptq @ t P R.
Definition 3.2 An extended real-valued function D : Γ R Ý Ñ R respects stochastic dominance (is a
D-parameter) if DpF q ě DpF 1q whenever F stochastically dominates F 1.

It can be noticed that the identification region for Qαrgpyqs shifts to the right as α increases.
The use of quantiles and their partial identifiability are relevant for analyzing the so-calledscore equat-
ing problem in educational measurement. Score equating is essential for any testing program that contin-
ually produces new editions of a test and for which the expectation is that scores from these editions have
the same meaning over time. Different editions may be built to a common blueprint and be designed to
measure the same constructs (mathematics ability, for instance), but they almost invariably differ some-
what in their psychometric properties (for instance, difficulties of the items). If one edition is more
difficult than another, examinees would be expected to receive lower scores on the harder form. Score
equating seeks to eliminate the effects on scores of these unintended differences in test form difficulty.
Score equating is necessary to be fair to examinees and to provide score users with scores that mean the
same thing across different editions or forms of the test.
Following Lord (1950) and Angoff (1984), “two scores, one on Form X and the other on Form Y
[. . .] may be considered equivalent if their corresponding percentile ranks in any given group are equal”
(Angoff, 1984, p.86). The equating transformation accordingly establishes equated scores as those that
have the same percentile rank in a given group of test takers. More precisely, let X P X and Y P Y be
the random variables representing test scores from tests forms X and Y , respectively; and letFX and FY
be their respective distribution functions. The X- and Y-scores are comparable if for each x P X , there
exists a unique y P Y such that
FX pxq “ P pX ď xq “ p “ P pY ď yq “ FY pyq, p P p0, 1q,
namely an equality of quantiles. In Chapter 9 we analyze this problem, making explicit the underlying
identification problem.
3.4 Combining Multiple Sampling Processes
The previous considerations implicitly assume that the available empirical evidence is generated by one
random sampling process. It is relevant to consider the case in which the empirical evidence is generated
by multiple sampling sampling processes. Each sampling process draws statistical units at random from
population J, and each yields some observable outcomes. Outcomes that are observable under some
sampling processes may be missing under others. The objective is to combine the data generated by the
sampling processes to identifyP pyq. The possibility of combining data from multiple sampling processes
arise often in surveys: one survey of a population of interest may attempt to interview respondents face-
to-face, another by mail or e-mail, etc. Each interview mode yield its own pattern of nonresponse.
In order to identify P pyq, let M denote the set of sampling processes. For each j P J and m P M, let
zjm “ 1 if yj is observable under sampling process m, and zjm “ 0 otherwise. It should be noticed that
this means that all the statistical units can be interviewed by each survey. For each m P M, the Law of
Total Probability gives
P pyq “ P py | zm “ 1qP pzm “ 1q ` P py | zm “ 0qP pzm “ 0q (3.4.7)
and therefore
P pyq P t P py | zm “ 1qP pzm “ 1q ` γmP pzm “ 0q, γ m P Γu. (3.4.8)
30

===== PAGE 38 =====

This corollary provides conditions to get point identification: if SM “ 1 then P pyq is point identified.

3.6 Instrumental variables
It is relevant to make explicit the standard way to solve the identification problem in the sense of point
identifying the distribution P pyq. As a matter of fact, distributional assumptions may enable one to
shrinks identification regions obtained using empirical evidence alone. This shows how strong could be
the distributional assumptions in such a way that the conclusions highly depend on them rather than on
the empirical evidence alone. A tension between credibility of such additional assumptions and stronger
conclusions becomes explicit, which is stated through theLaw of Decreasing Credibility(Manski, 2003):
Inference using the empirical evidence alone sacrifices strength of conclusions in order to maximize
credibility. Inference invoking point-identifying distributional assumptions sacrifices credibility in
order to achieve strength conclusions.
Between these two poles, there is a vast middle ground of possible modes of inference asserting assump-
tions that may shrink the identification region HrP pyqs but not reduce it to a point.
3.6.1 Preliminaries
Each person j P J is characterized by a covariate v P V . Moreover, for each j P J, yj is observable
if z “ 1 and it is unobservable if z “ 0. We will assume that all the realizations of v are observable.
The basic idea is that the observability of v provides an instrument that may help to identify P pyq. The
sampling process reveals the distributions
P pzq, P py, v | z “ 1q, P pv | z “ 0q.
It is uninformative about
tP py | v “ v, z “ 0q, v P V u.
3.6.2 Distributional assumptions
The following distributional assumptions are typically used in empirical research:
1. Outcomes missing-at-random (MAR): y K Kz | v, that is,
P py | vq “ P py | v, z “ 0q “ P py | v, z “ 1q.
34

===== PAGE 42 =====
2. Statistical independence of outcomes and instrument (SI): y K Kv, that is,
P py | vq “ P pyq.
3. Mean missing-at-random (MMAR): gpyq K z | v, where ¨ K ¨ | ¨ denotes conditional orthogo-
nality (for definition and properties, see Florens & Mouchart, 1985); that is,
Ergpyq | vs “ Ergpyq | v, z “ 1s “ Ergpyq | v, z “ 1s.
4. Mean independence of outcomes and instrument (MI): gpyq K Kv, that is,
Ergpyq | vs “ Ergpyqs.
5. Mean missing monotonically (MMA):
Ergpyq | v, z “ 1s ě Ergpyq | vs ě Ergpyq | v, z “ 0s.
6. Mean monotonicity of outcome and instrument (MM): Let V be an ordered set, then
Ergpyq | v “ vs ě Ergpyq | v “ v1s @p v, v1q P V ˆ V such that v ě v1.
Researchers contemplating the use of instrumental variables should pay due attention to the credibility
of these assumptions. Credibility depends not on the covariate per-se, but on the assumption that the
distribution P py, z, vq is assumed to satisfy.

propositions summarize the main conclusions of the distributional assumptions:

3.7 Concluding remark
In this chapter we discussed the problem of missing outcomes as an identification problem. We provided
two types of results: the first one is based on the empirical evidence alone and this leads us to obtain
identification regions for Ergpyqs. The width of the identification regions essentially depends on P pz “
1q. The second type of results are based on distributional assumptions which in general allow us to point
identify Ergpyqs. Both types of results show the trade-off underlying the Law of Decreasing Credibility.
In empirical research, this type of results compels us to make explicit the assumptions we are ready to
belief in order to solve identification problems.
38

===== PAGE 46 =====
Chapter 4
Conditional Prediction with Missing Data
In empirical research, we focus our attention on the conditional probability P py | xq, where y is an
outcome of interest and x a vector of covariates. Nevertheless, the presence of missing data induces
identification problems for this distribution. Following Manski (2005b), we study this problem, con-
sidering three missing-data patterns: missing outcomes, jointly missing outcomes and covariates, and
missing covariates.
More specifically, letX be the space of covariates. Each member of the population is characterized by
py, xq P Y ˆ X with distribution P py, xq. The objective is to learn on P py | x “ xq for x P X when
the realizations of py, xq may be observable in whole, in part, or not at all. Two binary random variables
pzy, zxq indicate observability. A realization of y is observable if zy “ 1 but not if zy “ 0. A realization
of x is observable if zx “ 1 but not if zx “ 0. The sampling process reveals distributions P pzx, zyq,
P py, x | zy “ 1, zx “ 1q, P py | zy “ 1, zx “ 0q and P px | zy “ 0, zx “ 1q. The problem is to use this
empirical evidence to identify P py | x “ xq, x P X. In what follows, it is assumed that X is finite and
that P px “ x, zx “ 1q ą 0 for all x P X.
4.1 Missing outcomes
The results obtained in Chapter 3 apply immediately to P py | x “ xq if realizations of x are always
observable. Thus,
HrP py | x “ xqs “ t P py | x “ xqP pzy “ 1 | x “ xq ` γ P pzy “ 1 | x “ xq, γ P ΓY u. (4.1.1)
4.2 Jointly missing outcome and covariates
Jointly missing outcomes and covariates is a regular occurrence in surveys: realizations of py, xq may
be missing in their entirety when sample members refuse to be interviewed or cannot be contacted by
survey administrators. Joint missingness also occurs when outcomes are missing and the objective is to
learn a distribution of the form P py | y P Bq, where B Ă Y .

4.5 Examples
4.5.1 Motivation
Official statistics are based on survey data 1. Such statistics are typically reported as point estimates,
without accompanying measures of error or indicators of reliability. Sometimes, official technical reports
acknowledge that official statistics are subject to error but they provide limited guidance on the magnitude
of sampling errors and typically do not attempt to quantify nonsampling errors. As Manski (2016)
mentions, prominent examples include the reporting of employment and income statistics by the Bureau
of Labor Statistics (BLS) and the Census Bureau:
• On the first Friday of each month, the BLS issues The Employment Situation, a monthly news
release reporting official employment statistics for the previous month. The unemployment-rate
1This section is based on Manski (2016).
44

===== PAGE 52 =====
statistic is based on data on households sampled in the Current Population Survey (CPS). The one
on nonfarm employment is based on data collected from employer establishments sampled in the
Current Employment Statistics survey (CES). The employment statistics are reported as point esti-
mates, without measures of potential error. A Technical Note issued with the news release contains
a section on Reliability of the estimates that acknowledges the possible presence of errors, begin-
ning with the statement “Statistics based on the household and establishment surveys are subject
to both sampling and nonsampling error”. The section describes the conventional use of standard
errors and confidence intervals to measure sampling error, providing some numerical illustrations.
The Technical Note then turns to nonsampling errors, stating that they “can occur for many rea-
sons, including the failure to sample a segment of the population, inability to obtain information
for all respondents in the sample, inability or unwillingness of respondents to provide correct in-
formation on a timely basis, mistakes made by respondents, and errors made in the collection or
processing of the data”.
• Each year the Census Bureau reports statistics on the income distribution based on data collected
in the Annual Social and Economic (ASEC) Supplement to the CPS. The Census release provides
point estimates, acknowledged but did not quantify sampling error, and did not mention nonsam-
pling error. The Census Bureau’s P60 Current Population Report provides numerous statistics
characterizing the income distribution and measures sampling error by providing 90% confidence
intervals for various estimates. However, the report does not measure nonsampling errors. A
supplementary technical document describes some sources of nonsampling error, but it does not
quantify them. Reporting official statistics as point estimates manifests a common tendency of pol-
icy analysts to project incredible certitude, encouraging the public to believe that errors are small
and inconsequential (Manski, 2013c). In the absence of agency guidance, persons who under-
stand that official statistics are subject to error must fend for themselves and conjecture the error
magnitudes. Thus, users of official statistics may misinterpret the information that the statistics
provide.
One of the nonsampling errors is due to survey nonresponse. Statistical agencies use traditional as-
sumptions –namely that nonresponse is random conditional on specified observed covariates– to form
point estimates with nonresponse. These assumptions are implemented as weights for unit nonresponse
and imputations for item nonresponse. In particular, the Census Bureau applies hot-deck imputations to
the CPS and other surveys, describing the hot deck this way:
This method assigns a missing value from a record with similar characteristics, which is the hot deck.
Hot decks are defined by variables such as age, race, and sex. Other characteristics used in hot decks
vary depending on the nature of the unanswered question. For instance, most labor force questions
use age, race, sex, and occasionally another correlated labor force item such as full- or part-time
status (U.S. Census Bureau, 2006, p.9).
45

===== PAGE 53 =====

4.5.2 Some findings
Official statistics describing income in the United States are based on data collected in the CPS. The ba-
sic CPS sampling unit is a household, which may include one or more families or unrelated individuals
who reside together. Data on annual income of household members in the preceding calendar year are
collected in the ASEC Supplement, which is administered in the period February through April. Data
on the current employment status of civilian adult household members are collected in the monthly ad-
ministration of the CPS. Applying the findings of this chapter, Manski (2016) uses ASEC data collected
in 2002-2012 to form interval estimates of median household. Table 4.1 shows the interval estimates: in
the absence of assumptions, the lower (upper) bound on median household income would be the value
that the median would take if all households with missing data were to have zero (infinite) income. It
can be appreciated how unreliable are the point estimates with imputation due to the nonresponses rates.
In any case, such estimates are a plausible value for the median household income because they belong
to the identification region.

===== PAGE 54 =====
Chapter 5
Dissecting Chilean Surveys: The Missing
Outcomes Case
Broadly speaking1, public surveys are applied either to get a better gauge of citizens’ political opinions
(Berinsky, 2017) or to collect information that is useful for policy makers. These surveys are perceived
as reliable tools as it is argued that they are applied to “representative samples”. If this were the case, the
analysis of the strength of a survey would be reduced to indicating how a sample design ensures access
to a “representative sample”. However, it is necessary to emphasize that the expression “representative
sample” is not a statistical concept because it is logically contradictory. As a matter fact, a survey is
applied to know the behavior of a population in relation to an outcome of interest. Doing so means that
we have no idea about this outcome: how then can we ensure the representativeness of the survey? On
the other hand, if we know this outcome, why do we need to conduct a survey?
A question then arises: how can we assess a survey? This chapter intends to answer this question in a
specific but quite typical case, namely when some surveyed individuals do not answer a specific question.
Our approach is based on the tension between the following two questions: what can be learned from
the data provided by a survey?; and, what do we want to learn from those data? The difference between
these two question relies on the statistical concept of identifiablity.
As a matter of fact, a statistical model is a family of probability distributions indexed by a parameter
and defined on a sample space; that is,
E “ tpM, Mq, P θ : θ P Θu
where pM, Mq is a measurable space, called sample space; P θ is a probability distribution defined on
pM, Mq and indexed by the parameter θ; and Θ is the set of plausible values of θ, called parameter
space. It should be remarked that this structure is not only valid for the so-called parametric models, but
also for semi-parametric and non-parametric models. Thus, in particular, a probability distribution can
1This chapter reproduces the paper entitled Dissecting Chilean Surveys: The Missing Outcomes Case by E. San Mart ´ın
and E. Alarc ´on-Bustamante, recently submitted to the Chilean Journal of Statistics . This journal was chosen by intellectual
coherence.
47

===== PAGE 55 =====
also be a parameter; for details, see San Mart´ın, Gonz´alez, and Tuerlinckx (2015).
From a modelling point of view, a set of data is fully represented by a probability distribution that
generates them. Consequently, a parameter of this distribution represents a specific characteristic of the
set of data under analysis; see Fisher (1922). Technically speaking, these correspond to the identified
parameter. However, if we attribute a characteristic to a set of data that cannot be represented by a
parameter (that is, it is not a functional of the probability distribution underlying the data), then we face
an identification problem. Technically speaking, these correspond to a parameter of interest. Thus, the
identified parameters summarize what can be learned from the data, whereas the parameters of interest
represent what we want to learn from the data. When an injective relationship is established between
them, then the identification problem is solved. For details and references, see Koopmans and Reiersøl
(1950), San Mart´ın (2018) and San Mart´ın and Gonz´alez (2022).
In this chapter we will use this distinction to assess both the strengths and weaknesses of three Chilean
surveys: two of political opinion (CADEM survey and Araucan ´ıa citizen consultation), and one related
to the income distribution of employees (CASEN). We will analyze the identification problem raised by
missing outcome. To do that, we will use Manski (2007a)’s technique of partial identification, which
allows us to evaluate how strong are the ignorability conditions typically used to impute missing data.
Based on this discussion, we will emphasize the way in which these survey should report their results.
This chapter is organized as follows. In Section 5.1 the political opinion survey CADEM is analyzed.
Section 5.2 focus its attention on the National Socioeconomic Characterisation Survey CASEN. Finally
Section 5.3 analyze a recent citizen consultation applied in the Araucan´ıa region in Chile. In each of these
sections, we provide the corresponding methodological information of each survey and also the political
and/or economical context in which the survey is used. The chapter ends by a general discussion.
5.1 CADEM survey
5.1.1 General objective and methodological information
According to the information provided on its website, the CADEM survey is one of the many services
offered by the market research company CADEM research & estrategia. Specifically, it is related to the
service called Plaza P´ublica, which describes itself as “the first and only polling platform that measures
public opinion on a weekly basis to provide data and analysis on a wide range of topics of interest” 2.
This particular aspect is related to one of the general objectives of this marketing company: “We want
to connect people with decision makers, through data and not from intuition, providing strategies and
action plans to achieve the expected results based on a deep knowledge of the new consumer/citizen”3.
CADEM survey delivers “reliable, timely and contingent information on the political, economic and
social debate in Chile on a weekly basis”. The study published by CADEM “contemplates a probabilistic
survey of 700 weekly cases (with a monthly consolidation that goes from 2800 surveys to 3500 depending
on whether the month has 4 or 5 weeks), applied 100% through cell phones, using CADEM’s own
2Retrieved from https://cadem.cl/sobre-cadem/ on December 30, 2021.
3Retrieved from https://cadem.cl/plaza-publica/ on December 30, 2021.
48

===== PAGE 56 =====
database that contains more than 18 million cell phones considering both prepaid and postpaid numbers,
all obtained through Random Digit Dialing and consolidated during the last four years”. Its target group
is, therefore, all individuals living in the national territory, Chileans and immigrants, men and women
over 18 years old, inhabitants of the 15 regions of the country. This led to perform a previous stratification
of the total population based on the population projections made by the National Institute of Statistics
(NIS) of the Chilean Government for the year 2017 at the national level. Table 5.1 presents the estimated
population aged 18 and over for each region of the country as of 2017 and the number of surveys proposed
for each region in order to comply with the national proportionality. In addition to the distribution by
region, the previous stratification considers, only as a control, the combination of sex and age variables;
for more details, see CADEM (2018).

It is important to emphasize that this general information is not published week by week, except for
the total number of people selected and the total number of people who agreed to answer the survey.
5.1.2 How are the missing responses treated?
Taking into account that the survey is conducted by telephone, the main issue is the non-response rate.
CADEM is not only aware of this problem, but distinguishes three cases of non-response: cases of no
contact, namely no one answers the call either because the phone is busy or out of service; cases of a
non-eligible person, namely a person who answers the call, but does not satisfy the requirements of the
49

===== PAGE 57 =====
target group; and a person who is correctly selected but refuses to answer the survey. The impact of the
non-response rate is assessed in the following terms:
Estimating the magnitude of non-response is critical because of the direct relationship it may have
with self-selection biases in public opinion polls. The calculation of the non-response rate is also used
as a measure of validation of the results. Under the assumption that those who rejects to answer the
survey are equal to those who answers it, the magnitude of the non-response rate does not offer major
disadvantages, but when there is evidence that the two groups are not equivalent, the non-response
can introduce serious distortions in the results (CADEM, 2018).
CADEM accordingly reports the rate of non-response. Three types of results are reported by the survey:
those that make explicit the number of cases surveyed, which is approximately equal to 700; those that
use a subset of these cases; and trends over time, using previous survey results. However, the impact of
the non-response rate on both the results of the survey and their report are not discussed. For an example,
see the survey published on the fourth week of December 2021 (CADEM, 2022).
5.1.3 Dissecting the CADEM survey
The objective of this section is to answer the following questions: What can be learned from the data
collected by the CADEM survey? How reliable is the CADEM survey? In order to be consistent with a
certain degree of reliability, how should its results be communicated?
Example
Let us consider the collected results during the fifth week of December 2021 (CADEM, 2022). As
mentioned above, each study contains a methodological sheet, which indicates that the sampling is a
probability sample with random selection of individuals and previously stratified by region; that the
sample consists of 705 cases, which required making 6,401 telephone calls, so the response rate is equal
to 11%. Let us focus our attention on the first question of the study:
Do you have a very positive, positive, negative or very negative image of Gabriel Boric?
The results are the following: 63% have a very positive or positive (denoted by a) image of Gabriel
Boric; 27% have a negative or very negative (denoted byb) image; and 10% do not know or non-response
(denoted by c).

P pY “ y | S “ 1, C “ 1q “ P pY “ y | S “ 1, C “ 0q for all y P ta, b, cu,

Y K KC | tS “ 1u; (5.1.5)
here V K KW | Z corresponds to the conditional independence between V and W given Z; for details
and properties on conditional independence, see Florens, Mouchart, and Rolin (1990, Chapter 2). This
condition, typically known as missing at random (?, ?; R. J. Little & Rubin, 2019), is not empirically
refutable because it depends on the component P pY “ y | S “ 1, C “ 0q which in turn is not based on
actual observations. Consequently, it is impossible to find out evidence establishing that “the two groups
are not equivalent”.
Correctly stated, condition (5.1.5) is an identification restriction (San Mart´ın & Gonz´alez, 2022) under
which P pY “ y | S “ 1q is point identified in the sense that
P pY “ y | S “ 1q “ P pY “ y | S “ 1, C “ 1q for all y P ta, b, cu.
In other words, under assumption (5.1.5), the uncertainty induced by the non-response decreases from an
interval of width P pC “ 0 | S “ 1q to the singleton tP pY “ y | S “ 1, C “ 1qu. Thus, what we want
to learn from the data coincides with what we can learn from the data . In passing, let us mention that
condition (5.1.5) should be viewed as a characterization of absence of (self-)biased and, consequently,
the identification problem induced by the non-response is exactly the same as the identification problem
induced by self-selection.

ď P pY “ y | S “ 1, C “ 1, G “ gqP pC “ 1 | S “ 1, G “ gq ` P pC “ 0 | S “ 1, G “ gq.
Therefore, the random selection of each individual in each stratum is far from helping to identifyP pY “
y | E “ 1, R “ 1q. Furthermore, it does not help to identify P pY “ y | E “ 1q either, since
P pY “ y | E “ 1, R “ 0q is still unidentified.
56

===== PAGE 64 =====
5.1.4 Discussion
CADEM research & estrategia offers services that “connect people with decision makers, through data
and not from intuition”. With regard to the CADEM survey, once it has been dissected, we find that the
motto is not entirely transparent. Indeed, what makes this survey a disservice is that the way in which
the results are reported hides the reliability of the results. However, the reliability of the results depends
on the parameter of interest to be reported.
The first level of analysis corresponds to a description of the collected data. For the sake of trans-
parency, CADEM must not only remember for each question of the survey the total number of people
who answered it, but also indicate, together with the percentages of preference for each option, the ab-
solute frequencies. This will warn the readers and especially the press that the results reflect the opinion
of a very small number of people . The second level of analysis makes explicit the uncertainty induced
by the non-response. CADEM should be made explicit such uncertainty by reporting both the lower
and the upper bound of the identification region of P pY “ y | S “ 1q. In the example, the impact of
the non-response rate can be appreciated, which prevents the reader from a false illusion of certainty. It
should be emphasized that condition (5.1.5) is a plausible way to treat the non-responses. A transparent
treatment of non-response should shows the impact of such a condition on the conclusions of the study.
As we have seen in the example, the conclusion depends much more on (5.1.5) than on the data itself.
The third level of analysis focuses on the eligible population. Once again, for the sake of transparency,
it is necessary to report both the lower and the upper bound of the identification region. The example
we have used shows how uninformative the survey results are. This information is more than relevant,
showing the intrinsic limits of this type of public opinion instruments.
5.2 CASEN survey
The National Socioeconomic Characterisation Survey (CASEN, for their initials in Spanish) is a Chilean
household survey that has been applied since 1987. It is used to assess the impact of social programs
on the living conditions of the population4. According to the Technical data sheet, the target population
is the population residing in private households throughout the national territory. The units of analysis
are families and individuals living in a household. A suitable respondent is the head of household or,
alternatively, a man or woman over 18 years old.
The sampling process of the CASEN survey consists of two steps. First, blocks are chosen that cor-
respond to sets of households; second, a household is chosen in which individuals are surveyed. Due
to the pandemic by COVID19, the last version of the survey, called 2020 CASEN survey in pandemic ,
was carried out in two steps: first, from the households selected in the previously mentioned sampling
process, a face-to-face pre-contact was applied to obtain a contact telephone number. Second, the survey
was administered by telephone.
In the 2020 CASEN in pandemic survey, 97.848 households were pre-contacted. Of these, only 86.189
households provided at least a telephone number to be contacted. Of these, 62.540 households had
4Retrieved from http://casenpandemia2020.cl/ on December 30, 2021
57

===== PAGE 65 =====
individuals who answered the survey, which amounted to 185.437 individuals 5. It should be remarked
that the available CASEN data set contains information on these individuals6.
5.2.1 Treatment of missing outcomes in the CASEN survey
One of the objectives of the CASEN survey is to obtain an overview of the income distribution in Chile,
and in particular to have an overview of poverty in the country in terms of income. However, the selected
individuals did not answer one of its questions, specifically the income question. CASEN considers ap-
propriate to impute these missing data, so that researchers and policy makers can use a database without
missing data. The chosen imputation procedure is called Conditional Mean Imputation. The rationale
of this technique can be summarized as follows: first, observed covariates are used to define classes.
Second, individuals who did not report their income and individuals who reported it are classified in the
same class if they share the characteristics of that class. For example, those people from city A, with
an age range 30-35 years old who do not report the income, are classified in the same class as those
people from the same city in the same age range that report the income. Third, it is computed the mean
of the observed incomes conditionally on a class: the missing incomes are imputed through this mean
(R. J. Little & Rubin, 2019).
More precisely, let Y be an outcome of interest, and let X be a set of fully observed covariates which
are used to define the classes. Let Z be a binary random variable such that Z “ 1 if the outcome is
observed, and Z “ 0 if not. The conditional mean of both respondents and non-respondents in the same
class are given by EpY | X “ x, Z “ 1q and EpY | X “ x, Z “ 0q, respectively. The Conditional
Mean Imputation assumes that, for each x,
EpY | X “ x, Z “ 0q “ EpY | X “ x, Z “ 1q. (5.2.1)
This assumption is also typically known asMean Missing at Random(Manski, 2007a), Weak Ignorability
(Imbens, 2000; Hirano & Imbens, 2004), and is equivalent to the conditional orthogonality between Y
and Z given X. For details on conditional orthogonality, see Florens and Mouchart (1982).
5.2.2 Dissecting the CASEN survey
Example
Let us focus our attention on the incomes of the salaried employees. According to the technical report
Measuring income and poverty in Chile, 2020 Casen Survey in Pandemic 7, 45.642 individuals were
considered in this category. These individuals were exposed to the following question:
The last month, what was your net income at your main job?

Remark 5.2 Let Y be a real random variable. The quantile function is defined as
qX pαq “ inf tt P R : P pY ď tq ě αu for α P r0, 1s.
This corresponds to the generalized inverse of the cumulative distribution function of Y ; see Embrechts
and Hofert (2013). The quantiles reported in Table 5.2, as in other part of this paper, were calculated
using this definition (for a code, see San Mart ´ın and Alarc´on-Bustamante (2022)), which respects the
nature of the data (the income is a discrete random variable), and not using the Hyndman and Fan
(1996)’s recommendations which is used, for instance, in R Core Team (2020). ■
Table 5.2 shows the impact of the imputation procedure on the quantiles of the income distribution.
How relevant is this impact on a global view of income distribution and poverty? Could we say that it is
negligible? These questions can be answered by addressing the following one: what can we learn about
the income by using the empirical evidence only? The remaining of this section is devoted to answer this
question.

0 if not.
• Zpmq “ 1 if the individual m reports the income, and Zpmq “ 0 if not.
• Let Y pmq be the income of individual m.

(i) tm P M : Spmq “ 1u Ă t m P M : Cpmq “ 1u;
(ii) tm P M : Zpmq “ 1u Ă t m P M : Spmq “ 1u X tm P M : Cpmq “ 1u.
(5.2.2)

First level of analysis
The first level corresponds to what we can learn from the data. This level is accordingly captured by the
identified parameters above described. Regarding the distribution of the reported incomes, Figure 5.1
shows that the slope of the curve rapidly increases for lower incomes. As a matter of fact, until 75%
of the salaried employees, there are non-dramatic changes in the income, so there is a low variability.
In contrast, in the 25% of employees with highest incomes this slope increase slowly, which means that
there is a great variability among the incomes.
Second level of analysis: Surveyed salaried employees
The second level of analysis is focused on the parameter of interest P pY ď y | C “ 1, S “ 1q, that is,
the income distribution of the salaried employees who answered the survey. The objective of this section
is to make explicit the impact of the non-response rate on the income distribution, the average income
and the corresponding quantiles. By doing so, it will be appreciated how strong is the Conditional Mean
Imputation implemented by the CASEN survey.
Income distribution:
Let us start by the income distribution. Using the Law of Total Probability, we have that
P pY ď C “ 1, S “ 1q “ P pY ď y | C “ 1, S “ 1, Z “ 1qP pZ “ 1 | C “ 1, S “ 1q `
(5.2.3)
P pY ď y | C “ 1, S “ 1, Z “ 0qP pZ “ 0 | C “ 1, S “ 1q.
In this decomposition, both P pY ď y | C “ 1, S “ 1, Z “ 1q and P pZ “ z | C “ 1, S “ 1q,
z P t0, 1u, are identified, whereas P pY ď y | C “ 1, S “ 1, Z “ 0q is not identified because it depends
61

===== PAGE 69 =====
on the employees who did not report their income. Instead of using an ignorability condition (as the
Conditional Mean Imputation), the relevant question is what can be learned about P pY ď| C “ 1, S “
1q without introducing additional assumptions. Taking into account that P pY ď y | C “ 1, S “ 1, Z “
0q P r 0, 1s, it is possible to bound P pY ď y | C “ 1, S “ 1q as follows:
P pY ď y | C “ 1, S “ 1, Z “ 1qP pZ “ 1 | C “ 1, S “ 1q ď
ď P pY ď| C “ 1, S “ 1q ď (5.2.4)
ď P pY ď y | C “ 1, S “ 1, Z “ 1qP pZ “ 1 | C “ 1, S “ 1q ` P pZ “ 0 | C “ 1, S “ 1q,
where P pZ “ 1 | C “ 1, S “ 1q “ 0.866. This identification region, depicted in Figure 5.2, includes an
infinite number of income distributions that are compatible with the observations. Moreover, it reflects
the uncertainty induced by the non-response rate: in fact, the width of this interval is equal to the non-
response rate, namely P pZ “ 0 | C “ 1, S “ 1q.

provided the support of Y is bounded. Although theoretically the support of Y is bounded, in practice

Table 5.4 deserves the following comments:
1. For α smaller than the non-response rate, the income of employees can be much lower than the in-
come that can be deduced from the CASEN income distribution. In other words, the non-response
rate has such an impact that it is not possible to know how poor the “poorest of the income of
employees” are.
2. For α greater that the response rate, the income of employees can be much higher than the income
that can be deduced from the CASEN income distribution. In other words, the response rate has

such an impact that it is not possible to know how rich the “richer of the income of employees”
are.

intersects the identification region ofqY |C“1,S“1pα2q. This clearly increases the uncertainty of the
conclusions that can be drawn using the partially identified income distribution and which, on the
other hand, is rendered invisible when using the CASEN income distribution.

The previous conclusions allow us to understand the meaning of ignorability conditions, such as the
Conditional Mean Imputation technique or, more generally, Missing at Random conditions. These con-
ditions come from the identification restriction
Y K KZ | C “ 1, S “ 1, X
which, by definition of conditional independence, is equivalent to
P pY ď y | C “ 1, S “ 1, Xq “ P pY ď y | C “ 1, S “ 1, Z “ 1, Xq
“ P pY ď y | C “ 1, S “ 1, Z “ 0, Xq.
These equalities means that the missing observations do not provide more relevant information about the
output Y , being the only “statistical job” to carefully estimate P pY ď y | C “ 1, S “ 1, Z “ 1, Xq
–which is the standard procedure.
5.2.3 Discussion
One of the objectives of the CASEN survey is to obtain an overview of the income distribution of em-
ployees and, in particular, to have a look at the incomes of the lowest paid employees as well as those of
the highest paid. For this purpose, the self-reported income of survey respondents who fall into the cate-
gory of salaried employees is used. However, individuals who are exposed to the survey are not required
65

===== PAGE 73 =====
to report their income. As a consequence, the survey includes a non-response rate which, for the 2020
CASEN survey in pandemic, is equal to 11.4456%. Before providing an overview of the distribution
of incomes, CASEN applies statistical techniques designed to impute missing income, specifically the
Conditional Mean Imputation technique.
Our dissection of the CASEN survey aims to make explicit the policy meaning of this imputation
technique. To this end, a partial identification analysis was developed to show the impact of the non-
response rate on both the mean of the distribution of the income distribution of employees and on the
respective quantiles. One of the main conclusions is that “the poor may be poorer” than what can be
asserted from the CASEN income distribution, and that “the rich may be richer” than what can be stated
from it.
With this conclusion in mind, it is possible to assess the sense of the imputation technique used by
CASEN: the Conditional Mean Imputation technique corresponds to an assumption of income homo-
geneity. As a matter of fact, it is assumed that, among individuals with characteristics X “ x who did
not report their income, there is no relevant income information that was not accessed: all the effectively
relevant information has already been observed in those who did report their income. Consequently, the
income of an employee who did not report it should be related to the average income of all employees
sharing the same characteristics X “ x. The partial identification analysis show how heterogenous may
be the indome distributions of employees. Policy decisions should be aware on this uncertainties.
5.3 The Araucan ´ıa citizen consultation
5.3.1 Historical and economical context
The capital of the Araucan´ıa region, Temuco, is located 620 kilometers to south of Santiago, the capital
of Chile. The Araucan ´ıa Region is known for being the original area of the Mapuche People (in the
16th century called ”Araucanos”), possibly the only indigenous people with whom the Crown of Spain
made a Capitulation of Peace, known as the Paces de Quil´ın, made on January 5 and 6, 1614. This
treaty established the B ´ıo B´ıo River as the border, south of which “the Mapuches lived independently
for two hundred and forty years, until 1881” (Bengoa, 2007). In 1881, “Manuel Recabarren, Minister
of the Interior [at the time], led Chilean troops to the south and, together with General Gregorio Urrutia,
advanced hundreds of kilometers along the border and militarily occupied the area” (Bengoa, 2016).
This completed the occupation of Araucan´ıa by the Chilean government.
The Araucan´ıa Region, in addition to the B´ıo B´ıo, Los R´ıos and Maule regions, develop the country’s
forestry industry: “the forestry sector represents 1.9% of the domestic GDP, reaching in 2017 USD 5,196
million (3,373 billion of Chilean pesos). B´ıo B´ıo region represents 60.0% of the forestry GDP, followed
by La Araucan´ıa region with 10.5%, and Los R´ıos, and Maule regions with 10.1% each. Regarding the
participation of the three forestry subsectors included in the sectorial GDP, the paper, and pulp industry,
as well as products derived from paper represents 44.3%, forestry participates with 29.4%, and the wood
products, and wood industry represent 26.3%” (Instituto Forestal, 2021).
Many of the conflicts in the area are due to the presence of forestry companies, whose worldview on
66

===== PAGE 74 =====
nature and its resources is not entirely shared by the Mapuche people’s worldview. In addition, part of
the forestry exploitation takes place on what were once Mapuche lands, which has triggered a series of
territorial claims (Andrade, 2019).
5.3.2 Recent political context
On October 12, 2021, the President of the Chilean Republic declared a state of emergency for the
provinces of B ´ıo B´ıo and Arauco, in the Biob ´ıo region, and in the provinces of Caut ´ın and Malleco,
in the Araucan´ıa Region, for a 15 days period (Diario Oficial de la Rep´ublica de Chile, 2021). According
to the Chilean Constitution, this is one of its prerogatives, and it may declare such state of emergency
for no more than 15 days. Once a state of emergency is declared, the respective zones will be under
the immediate dependence of the Chief of National Defense appointed by the President of the Republic,
who will assume the direction and supervision of his jurisdiction with the powers and obligations estab-
lished by law (Constituci ´on de la Rep ´ublica de Chile, 2005, At.42). By declaring a state of emergency,
the President of the Republic may restrict the freedom of locomotion and assembly (Constituci ´on de la
Rep´ublica de Chile, 2005, At.43).
Among the reasons that led to this decision, the Diario Oficial de la Rep´ublica de Chile (2021) mentions
the following ones:
1. An increase of violence acts linked to drug trafficking, terrorism and organized crime, committed
by armed groups that have not only made attempts on the lives of members of the Law Enforcement
and Security Forces, but have also attacked people and destroyed facilities and machinery used in
industrial, agricultural and commercial activities.
2. Since 2018, there has been an increase in crimes and offenses against persons and against property;
against public order, including attacks against authority, attacks and threats against prosecutors of
the Public Prosecutor’s Office and the Judiciary.
3. There has been a 116% increase in reported incidents related to crimes contemplated in Law No.
17.798 on Arms Control, including the seizure of weapons and ammunition.
4. The number, magnitude and seriousness of the crimes and facts indicated, committed in the
provinces of the regions of Biob ´ıo and Araucan´ıa, imply a serious alteration of public order –
understood as the “situation that allows the peaceful exercise of rights and the fulfillment of obli-
gations, ensuring peaceful coexistence”– in the terms established in Article 42 of the Constitution
of the Chilean Republic, which allows the enactment of the state of emergency constitutional ex-
ception with respect to such areas of the national territory, provided for in said article.
As it was mentioned above, the state of emergency may not be extended for more than fifteen days,
notwithstanding that the President of the Republic may extend it for the same period. However, for
successive extensions, the President shall always require the consent of the National Congress, specifi-
cally the Senate (Constituci´on de la Rep´ublica de Chile, 2005, Art.42). Until January 2022, the National
67

===== PAGE 75 =====
Congress has approved the extension of the state of emergency for 6 consecutive times 9. It should be
mentioned that the official account of the recent conflicts in La Araucan´ıa does not relate these conflicts
to the territorial claims of the Mapuche people.
5.3.3 Organization of the Araucan ´ıa consultation and results
In order to know the opinion of the citizens of the 32 communes of La Araucan ´ıa regarding the renewal
of the state of emergency in the region, the Regional Intendancy and the Association of Municipalities
of La Araucan´ıa organized a citizen consultation, which took place on November 5, 6 and 7, 2021. The
consultation was carried out electronically, and all persons over 18 years old registered in the electoral
registry in any of the 32 municipalities may participated from a computer, cell phone or another device
connected to the internet10.
The citizen consultation was limited to the following question:
Do you agree with Congress extending the state of emergency in the Araucan´ıa Region?
The results of the consutlation are summarized in Table 5.5.

5.3.4 How these results were used?
Sections 5.3.1 and 5.3.2 attempt to illustrate the complexity of the political situation in the Araucan ´ıa
region. This complex context may explain why successive extensions of the state of emergency have
been subject to lively debate. In fact, those extensions did not achieve unanimity in the Senate: they
were approved not more than 2 or 3 votes in favor. Let us mention the Senate session of November
24, 2021, where the extension of the state of emergency was approved by 16 votes in favor, 14 against,
and one abstention. Among the reasons that were mentioned for approving the extension, the Araucan´ıa
consultation was explicitly mentioned as an important factor. This was stated by Senator Francisco
Chahu´an, from the right coalition Chile Vamos, who affirmed that “the state of exception has generated
9For details, see https://www.senado.cl/senado/site/cache/search/pags/search164185188127928.html. Retrieved on January
10, 2021.
10Retrieved from https://www.consultaaraucania.cl/ on January 10, 2022.

===== PAGE 76 =====
greater tranquility. Attacks against property and arson crimes have decreased. We must listen actively
and in La Araucan´ıa there was a citizen consultation that supported this measure”11. These expressions
are in line with the assessment made by the Governor of La Araucan ´ıa, Luciano Rivas, independent,
near to the Chile Vamos coalition, at the end of the consultation: “With great respect, but also with great
strength, we ask politicians, especially all the deputies and senators of Chile, that our voice be heard, do
not turn a deaf ear”12.
As mentioned by Governor Rivas13, the Araucan´ıa citizen consultation was one of the first, if not the
first, non-binding consultations to be held in Chile. This, added to the complex political situation in the
Araucan´ıa region, could explain the interest that this consultation aroused, especially in the relationship
that its results have with recent elections, namely the 2020 national referendum on the possibility of a
new constitution and the 2021 governor elections. One of these studies is the one conducted by Cayul,
Dur´an, and Jaimovich (2021), which was initially published in the electronic journal CIPER 14. This
study analyzes the representativeness at the minicipality level of the Araucan ´ıa Consultation on three
axes: Mapuche population, rurality, and population that voted for the non-approval option in the 2020
national referendum. According to the authors, “these axes are fundamental to establish whether or
not there is a bias in the results, since it analyzes the cultural, socioeconomic and political dimension”.
To achieve this objective, the authors analyze, on the one hand, the participation in the second round
of the election of Regional Governors in Araucan ´ıa with the percentage of Mapuche population, the
percentage of rural population and the percentage of non-approval in the 2020 referendum; and, on the
other hand, the participation in the citizen consultation in Araucan ´ıa with the same percentages already
mentioned. The choice of the regional governors is due to the fact that in that election “a similar universe
of approximately 125,000 people participated”.
We are able to reproduce the third analysis by considering the data summarized at Table 5.6. Figures
5.4 and 5.5 reproduce their analysis. Cayul et al. (2021) conclude that “those municipalities with a higher
percentage of votes for the non-approval to a new Constitution also had a higher participation in both the
citizen consultation and in the second round of governors’ elections, but the effect is significantly lower
in the latter. That is, there would be a political bias of those who participate in the consultation”. The
final conclusion of the study is the following:
We observe then that, when comparing two elections with a similar participation rate, the
people who participate in them are very different. While participation in the consultation
was higher in urban, non-Mapuche municipalities that voted for non-approval, these same
biases are not observed in the second round of governors election.
electors, then, are not representative at the municipal level, and this suggests that the con-
sultation is not necessarily representative of the population of Araucan ´ıa. This implies that

5.3.5 Dissecting the use of Araucan ´ıa citizen consultation
Statement of the problem
The previous analysis consists in comparing two or more elections that share a common electoral roll.
Now, for each election, there are specific probability distributions that are identified, namely (i) the
distributions of participation and non-participation, and (ii) the distribution of preferences conditionally
on the electors participating in the election. More precisely, let M be the sample space composed of the
electors, and define the following random variables on M:
• V1pmq “ 1 if the elector m participated at the 2020 referendum, and V1pmq “ if not.
• V2pmq “ 1 if the elector m participated at the 2021 governor election, and V2pmq “ if not.
• V3pmq “ 1 if the elector m participated at the 2021 citizen consultation, and V3pmq “ if not.
• Let Y1 be the preference at the 2020 referendum, namelyY1 “ tapprove, non-approve, blank vote, null voteu.
• Let Y2 be the preference at the 2021 governor election, namelyY2 “ tTuma, Rivas, blank vote, null voteu.
• Let Y3 be the preference at the 2021 citizen consultation, namelyY3 “ tyes, no, blank vote, null voteu.
• Let C be the municipality in which each elector is registered. C takes 32 different values because
there are 32 municipalities; see Table 5.6.
If we consider each election separately, then the identified parameters are the following:
P pYi “ yi | Vi “ 1, C “ cq p yi, cq P Yi ˆ t1, . . . ,32u; P pVi “ 1 | C “ cq c P t1, . . . ,32u
for i “ 1, 2, 3.
If these elections are jointly used, the first thing to verify is if the set of electors is the same, that is, if
the following equality is present:
tm P M : V1pmq “ 1u Y tm P M : V1pmq “ 0u “
“ tm P M : V2pmq “ 1u Y tm P M : V2pmq “ 0u

plausible, which in turn shows that it is possible to have turnout rates in both elections lower than the
upper bound. By passing, this jeopardizes the argument according to which the governor election and
the citizen consultation can be compared because their rate of participation are similar.
