are quite small: 0.04 and 0.06, respectively.
5.3.6 Discussion
The partial identification analysis shows the impact of the uncertainty due to the joint participation in
both elections, namely 2020 referendum and 2021 consultation, on the proportion of electors who chose
the non-approve option at the 2020 referendum. This impact can be diminished if, for each municipality,
the joint distribution P pV1 “ v1, V3 “ v3 | C “ cq with pv1, v3q were known. This seems to be feasible
for the Chilean Electoral Service, without having to transgress elector identity protection. If this were
the case, then P pV3 “ 1 | V1 “ 1, C “ cq would be identified. However, this fact does not ensure that
P pY1 “ y | V1, “ 1, V3 “ 1, C “ cq is point identified because P pY1 “ y | V1 “ 1, V3 “ 0, C “ cq is
not identified given the secrecy of the vote. Consequently, following the arguments developed in Section
5.3.5, P pY1 “ y | V1, “ 1, V3 “ 1, C “ cq belongs to an identification interval with a lower bound given
by
max
"
0, P pY1 “ y | V1 “ 1, C “ cq ´ P pV3 “ 1 | V1 “ 1, C “ cq
P pV3 “ 0 | V1 “ 1, C “ cq
*
and an upper one given by
min
"
1, P pY1 “ y | V1 “ 1, C “ cq
P pV3 “ 0 | V1 “ 1, C “ cq
*
.
78

===== PAGE 86 =====
It can be deduced that this interval is informative (that is, strictly included in r0, 1s) if
P pV3 “ 1 | V1 “ 1, C “ cq ă P pV3 “ 0 | V1 “ 1, C “ cq,
which is a surprising result.
It could be argued that, under “mild conditions”, it is possible to ignore joint participation, and thus
argue for the reliability of studies such as the one reported in Section 5.3.4. The partial identification
analysis developed in Section 5.3.5 shows that there are two possible assumptions that could be made:
the first one would be to assume that P pY1 “ non-approve | V1 “ 1, V3 “ 0q “ 0, that is, that no
elector who participated in the 2020 referendum and did not participate in the 2021 citizen consultation
chose the option non-approve. It should be mentioned that this assumption is quite strong and hard to
believe. A second assumption would be Y1 K KV3 | tV1 “ 1u, C, which is equivalent to the following two
equivalent conditions:
P pY1 “ y | V1 “ 1, V3 “ 1, C “ cq “ P pY1 “ y | V1 “ 1, C “ cq;
P pV3 “ v3 | Y “ y, V1 “ 1, C “ cq “ P pV3 “ v3 | V1 “ 1, C “ cq v3 P t0, 1u.
The last condition means that, once an elector of a specific municipality participated at the 2020 referen-
dum, the participation at the 2021 consultation does not depend on the preference at the 2020 referendum:
again hard to believe.
Finally, it should be emphasized that the previous analysis of partial identification is applicable to
critically assess the comparisons over time of political surveys as they would be correctly done if made
conditionally on joint participation.
5.4 Concluding remarks
This paper illustrates a possible service that Applied Statistics can render to society. Both political
opinion polls and socioeconomic surveys have different uses. The former try to monitor political opinions
in relation to issues of citizen interest; the latter are especially used by policy makers who want to assess
policies or to have a global view of aspects such as poverty or income. In a democratic society these uses
are relevant because they can, for example, change political opinions or question the impact of certain
public policies. This is precisely the reason for assessing the strengths and weaknesses of the surveys.
From a statistical point of view, we focus our assessment of the surveys on the correct way of commu-
nicating their results, so that the uncertainty induced by non-responses is made explicit. The results can
be reported at different levels depending on the population of interest to which the results are to be gen-
eralized. The advantage of this strategy is that it makes explicit how this uncertainty could be reduced,
and which is impossible to reduce unless very strong assumptions are introduced, which make the con-
clusions not credible –that is, Law of Decreasing Credibility (?, ?). For instance, in the Araucan´ıa citizen
consultation, the uncertainty of the option at the 2020 referendum conditionally on the joint participation
at both the 2020 referendum and 2021 consultation can decreased whether the Chilean Electoral Service
provides information on such joint participation. However, the uncertainty can not decrease to a point
value because of the secrecy of the vote.
79

===== PAGE 87 =====
At the methodological level, the assessment or dissection of the Chilean surveys was performed making
a distinction between identified parameters and parameters of interest: what we can learn from the data
is represented by the identified parameters, while what we want to learn from the data is represented
by the parameters of interest. In (almost) all empirical research there is a gap between both types of
parameters; it is quite relevant to highlight the difference and to study their possible relationships –which
is equivalent to solving an identification problem. The way ? (?)expresses himself is illuminating and
perhaps summarizes the perspective developed in this paper:
Anyone who has tried to make sense to real data will, sooner or later, have come across the problem
of nonidentifiability. Broadly speaking this means that their first explanation of the data is not the
only one. The existence of alternative explanations becomes important when decisions have to be
made and particularly so when different explanations suggest completely different courses of action.
The identification regions we established for each of the Chilean survey contain such different explana-
tions.
80

===== PAGE 88 =====

In Section 5.1.3 we discuss some aspects related to the way in which surveys should be reported when
they are analyzed under a partial identification perspective. Let us come back to the example discussed
there. We defined a random variable y which can take three possible values:
• ypmq “ a if the participant m have a very positive or positive image of the Chilean President.
• ypmq “ b if the participant m have a negative or very negative image of the Chilean President.
• ypmq “ c if the participant m does not known;
here participant refers to a cell phone m associated to a person who answers the call, or not. According
to the available data, we have
P py “ a | s “ 1, c “ 1q “ 0.63, P py “ b | s “ 1, c “ 1q “ 0.27, P py “ c | s “ 1, c “ 1q “ 0.1,

cpmq “ 1 if the person associated with cell phone m answers the survey, and cpmq00 if not. Moreover,
P pc “ 1 | s “ 1q “ 0.11.

If we report for each possible value of y, then we have the following sentences:
• At least 6.93% of contacted people have a very positive or positive image of the Chilean president,

. . . and at least 2.97% have a negative or very negative image, and at least 1.1% don’t know
about the image of the Chilean president.

very negative image.
The idea of this report is to make explicit the uncertainty due to the missing data. However, it is hard
to understand.
6.2 Proposal of reporting
The idea of the above report is to make explicit the uncertainty due to missing data: it is the uncertainty of
the induction that is made taking into account the observed data and the population of interest on which
the induction is performed. In this section we will suggest an alternative way of reporting the results. To
do so, we note that
P py “ t | s “ 1, c “ 1qP pc “ 1 | s “ 1q “ P py “ t, c “ 1 | s “ 1q t P ta, b, cu.
The probability on the right-hand side of the above equality corresponds to the proportion of respondents
who chose level t for the random variable y and answered the survey. It should be emphasized that these
joint probabilities represent all what we observe. More precisely, P pc “ 0 | s “ 1q corresponds to the
proportion of selected person who refuses to answer the survey and, therefore
P pc “ 1 | s “ 1q “ 1 ´ P pc “ 0 | s “ 1q
is the proportion of persons who provides information. This proportion can be decomposed with respecto
to the random variable y, that is,
P pc “ 1 | s “ 1q “ P py “ a, c “ 1 | s “ 1q ` P py “ b, c “ 1 | s “ 1q `
P py “ c, c “ 1 | s “ 1q.
83

===== PAGE 91 =====
Each of the summands corresponds to the proportion of respondents who choose a level of y and agreed
to answer the survey. Furthermore, taking into acocunt that
1 “ P pc “ 1 | s “ 1q ` P pc “ 0 | s “ 1q
“ P py “ a, c “ 1 | s “ 1q ` P py “ b, c “ 1 | s “ 1q `
P py “ c, c “ 1 | s “ 1q ` P pc “ 0 | s “ 1q,
this decomposition reflects not only all the available information with respect to the sample space (popu-
lation of interest), but also reveals the uncertainty induced by the missing data throughP pc “ 0 | s “ 1q.
Note also that
P py “ t, c “ 1 | s “ 1q
corresponds to the lower bound of P py “ t | s “ 1q for each t P t a, s, cu. This decomposition can be
visualized as it is shown at Figure 6.1. This example shows how great is the uncertainty of the induction
made from the survey data: it can be seen at a glance, and therefore the report should consist of this type
of graphics.

7.1 Motivation
Social researchers1 typically use the term data errors to describe situation in which the available data
imperfectly measure variables of interest. Data errors produce identification problems, some of which
we will study in this chapter.
Data errors can be conceptualized through a mixture model, which views the available data as re-
alizations of a probability mixture of the variable of interest and another random variable. Let each
member j of population J have a pair of outcomes py˚

the probability mixture

those with z “ 1 are said to be error-free, and y itself is said to be a contaminated measure of y˚.

7.3 Contamination through imputation
Organizations conducting major surveys often impute for missing data and report statistics that are mix
of real and imputed data. Consider a researcher who sees the reported statistics but who does not see the
raw survey data and does not know the imputation rule used when data are missing. To this researcher,
imputation are data errors that may be analyzed using previous findings.
As it was mentioned in Section 4.5.1, data on the household income distribution in the U.S.A. are
collected annually in the Current Population Survey (CPS). Summary statistics are published by the U.S.
Bureau of the Census in Series P-60 of its Current Population Reports. Two sampling problems identified
by the Bureau of the Census are interview nonresponse, wherein some households in the CPS sampling
frame are not interviewed, and item nonresponse, wherein some of those interviewed do not provide
complete income responses. The Bureau of Census uses available information to impute missing income
data. The Bureau mixes actual and imputed data to produce the household income statistics reported in
its Series P-60 publications.
Using the notation introduced above, y˚ corresponds to the income a household selected for interview
in the CPS would report if it were to complete the survey, e is the income the Bureau would impute to
the household if the household were not to complete the survey, and z “ 1 if a CPS household actually
completes the survey. P py | z “ 1q is the distribution of income reported by those CPS households
88

===== PAGE 96 =====
who complete the survey, P py˚q is the distribution of income that would be reported if all households in
the CPS sampling frame were to complete the survey, and P pyq is the distribution of household income
found in the Series P-60 publications. The error probability p is the probability that a CPS household
does not complete the survey.
Consider the year 1989. U. S. Bureau of the Census states that in the March 1990 CPS, which provides
data on income during 1989, approximately 4.5% of the 60.000 households in the sampling frame were
not interviewed and that incomplete income data were obtained from approximately 8% of the persons in
interviewed households. The Bureau’s publication does not report how the latter group are spread across
households but we can be sure that no more than p0.08q ˆ p0.95q “ 0.076 of the households have item
nonresponse, so
λ “ 0.076 ` 0.045 “ 0.121
provides an upper bound on p.
Now consider P pyq. U. S. Bureau of the Census provides findings for each of 21 income intervals (in
thousands of dollars):

Chapter 8
A Qualitative Analysis of Peru’s 2021
Presidential Elections: A Partial
Identification Strategy
8.1 Introduction
Last June1, the Peruvian population went to the polls to cast their vote to elect the republic’s next presi-
dent. However, the election has been under the shadow of questioning due to allegations of fraud. Some
of the allegations of fraud point out that a candidate cannot obtain zero votes in a polling place. Besides,
it is claimed that an unusual number of votes favoring the candidate of the Per ´u Libre party occurred.
This situation has been observed in the frequency distribution of the votes cast. Other accusations are
related to the frequency with which extreme observations concerning the number of votes occur, i.e.,
polling places with all voters supporting a single candidate. However, neither of these arguments have
been proven from a technical point of view.
The main objective of this study is to propose, through a strategy of partial identification and parame-
terization of fraud, an analytical tool that allows us to put ourselves in different electoral scenarios. For
this, on the one hand, we make explicit the implications of having different levels of participation and,
therefore, what we can affirm regarding the electoral results. On the other hand, we develop a model
that incorporates different forms and amounts of electoral fraud in different contexts to generate concrete
hypotheses regarding the impact that the existence of electoral fraud may have.
8.2 Motivation
On May 18th, 2003, voters in Belgium went to the polls. V oting was done on a computer in many
regions, but the system had a backup. Late that night, as the votes were being tabulated, one of the
1This chapter reproduces a 2021-draft paper by Pablo Vargas, Ernesto San Mart´ın and Mauricio Castro.
90

===== PAGE 98 =====
election officials detected a problem with the results from Schaerbeek, a municipality in central Brussels.
Maria Vindevoghel, a little-known candidate with her party, received more votes than possible. The
officials started a votes recount, obtaining that the vote totals for every candidate were precisely the same
as before, except for Maria that was less than the original by 4,096. Everything about the hardware
seemed to be in perfect working order. Computers worked using binary strings of zeros and ones, each
corresponding to a power of two. A string of bits representing the number of votes Maria received was
somewhere inside the computer tabulating all the votes. It started the day all zeros, and then as each vote
for her came in, it would increment by one. Physically, this is done by turning on a transistor for one
and turning it off for zero. What is remarkable about the number 4096 is that it is exactly a power of
two (two to the power of twelve). That is the 13th bit so, for Maria Vindevoghel to receive an extra 4096
votes, the 13th bit had to flip from a zero to a one.
An investigation concluded that a single event upset (SEU) gave the candidate 4,096 extra votes. SEU
is a change of state caused by one single ionizing particle striking a sensitive node ( e.g., memory “bit”)
in the microelectronic device, a type of soft error. The error is soft because the device has not been
damaged, but the user could erase it and rewrite it with no problems. However, what happens if we do
not have a backup, or if it is a person with political biases who does the counting of the votes. The
fraud takes on a panoply of forms that we will deepen later. The question is whether we can determine
if a minor distortion can affect the elections’ outcome and if this constitutes a “soft error” that can be
corrected with no problems.
8.3 Background on electoral fraud
The study of electoral fraud is not something new. There are numerous contributions in the literature de-
voted to the statistical modeling of the behavior of an election to infer whether or not the results obtained
show signs of fraud. In other words, these electoral forensics approaches aim to detect manipulations
of ballots and the vote count. Leemann and Bochsler (2014) argue that a quickly growing literature has
been developed on two types of tools of vote forensics. One part of the literature discusses whether the
analysis of single digits of the reported electoral results at the ward level can reveal that these numbers are
based on the actual count of the votes, or whether they have been altered, relying on Benford’s law on the
frequency distribution of digits in numbers. The other part of the literature investigates the plausibility
of electoral results from wards and is based on detailed information.
Lehoucq (2003) proposes that there is a fine line between fraud and political pressure. To take a par-
ticularly glaring example, landlords are not guilty of electoral fraud when all retainers vote for their
party, regardless of whether peasants have been duped into voting in favor of the landlord’s party or
wish to avoid the reprisals associated with voting against their employer. Moreover, these activities are
not fraudulent unless a law has been broken, even if they are morally reprehensible. A vital advantage
of this legalistic conception of fraud is that it allows assessing the location of the boundaries between
acceptable and unacceptable political activity. The research on electoral fraud tells a consistent story.
First, fraud takes on a panoply of forms. It ranges from procedural violations of electoral law (that may
or may not intend to distort results) to the outright use of violence to intimidate voters and poll watchers.
91

===== PAGE 99 =====
Second, ballot-rigging does not appear to be decisive most of the time. The colorful history of vote
fabrication probably exaggerates its role in determining election outcomes. Fraud, nevertheless, under-
mines political stability because, in close races, it can be decisive. Third, political competition shapes
the rhythm and nature of electoral fraud. Studying electoral fraud is not easy because it is supposed to
remain clandestine. However, analyzing ballot rigging is far from impossible.
L´opez-Pintor (2010) refers to electoral fraud as deceptive or negligent interference with the electoral
process that intends to prevent the outcome from reflecting the will of the people. There are two main
categories of electoral fraud: outcome-determinative fraud, where the fraud affects the outcome of the
election such that the winners and losers are different from what they would have been had the fraud
not been committed; and nonoutcome-determinative fraud, when the outcomes are not affected (i.e. the
winners and losers would be the same even absent the commission of fraud). Both types of electoral fraud
entail criminal behavior that should be punished according to the law. However, outcome-determinative
fraud has more serious political implications in that it allows a party or candidate to take over public
positions contrary to the popular will.
For example, Hausmann and Rigobon (2011) have studied Venezuela’s Recall Referendum in 2004,
defining electoral fraud as the difference between the elector’s intent and the official vote tally. Since
the voters’ intent cannot be observed directly, the authors considered independent variables correlated
with the voter’s intent but not with the fraud. These variables were the number of signatures in favor
of holding a recall referendum and the proportion of yes responses in the exit polls. In this context, the
authors consider an instrumental variable approach where the intention of voters is used to model the
number of signatures in favor of holding a recall referendum and the proportion of yes responses.
In this thesis, we will talk about election forensics, which is the field devoted to using statistical
methods to determine whether the results of an election accurately reflect the intentions of the elec-
tors. Election forensics techniques apply statistical methods to low-level aggregates of votes such as
polling stations or ballot box counts of eligible voters and votes recorded for parties (Ferrari, McAlister,
& Mebane Jr, 2018). It is important to consider whether any of the “frauds” estimated represent gen-
uine frauds in the sense of maleficent acts arising from regular political activity such as strategic voting.
In some cases, we can draw on supplemental information to reach judgments, but in other cases, not
(Mebane Jr, 2016).
Another situation where the statistical analysis of the election intends to detect systematic fraud is
described by Klimek, Yegorov, Hanel, and Thurner (2012). In that paper, the authors developed a para-
metric model for quantifying systematic irregularities in an election process. This model includes two
parameters representing the incremental and extreme fraud situations. The first one refers to the situation
where votes (ballots) are taken away from both the nonvoters and the opposition, and they are added to
the winning party’s votes (ballots), while the latter one means that almost all votes (ballots) from the
nonvoters and the opposition are added to the winning party’s votes (ballots). In words of Ferrari et al.
(2018) two kinds of election fraud refer to how many of the opposition and nonvoters votes are shifted:
with ”incremental fraud,” moderate proportions of the votes are shifted; with ”extreme fraud,” almost
all of the votes are shifted. The model relies on the Gaussian assumption for the number of votes and
turnouts, and the conclusions are reported for different levels of data aggregation (territorial units).
92

===== PAGE 100 =====
8.3.1 Measuring electoral fraud
Not all authors agree with the same assumptions or methods to determine whether or not there was
electoral fraud and the magnitude. For example, Klimek et al. (2012) claim that it is not enough to
discuss the approximate normality of participation, vote, or logarithmic distributions of the voting rate to
decide whether the election results may be corrupt. These methods can lead to ambiguous signals because
the results depend to a great extent on the level of aggregation of the electoral data. Therefore, we can
agree that the levels of aggregation are a critical dimension to consider when analyzing the behavior of
electoral fraud. Ideally, the results and conclusions reached are robust and consistent with these different
levels of aggregation.
Other works focus more on a point estimation of electoral fraud than on determining whether a voting
process was fraudulent or not. For example, Christensen and Schultz (2014) state that election fraud
in established democracies typically involves only a tiny percentage of votes in a closely contested race.
Such a small number of fraudulent ballots can easily be concealed in a sea of otherwise legitimate ballots.
Those who steal elections rely on this fact to hide their activities. For example, they know that adding or
changing 10% or 20% of the ballots in a few precincts will not stand out as anomalous, making it nearly
impossible to differentiate legitimately cast votes from fraudulent votes.
Additionally, a recent study of Gonzalez (2021) examines the impact of cell phone access on elec-
tion fraud during the 2009 Afghan presidential election using a spatial regression discontinuity design.
Polling centers just inside coverage report a drop in the share of fraudulent votes of 4 percentage points,
while the likelihood of a fraudulent station decreases by eight percentage points. Their results provide
considerable evidence that cell phone coverage lowers electoral fraud. It is important to stress that the
estimated effects exhibit a considerable degree of spatial heterogeneity: average impacts in the south and
east are economically and statistically significant while non-distinguishable from zero in other parts of
the country. These results indicate that there may be patterns or spatial characteristics related to elections
that strongly mimic the observed pattern of fraud.
8.3.2 Other perspectives
Some studies analyze electoral fraud from other perspectives somewhat different from those previously
exposed. For example, the work of Ichino and Sch ¨undeln (2012) extends the empirical scholarship on
electoral fraud to the study of misconduct at the pre-election stage. It presents findings from a randomized
field experiment on the effect of domestic observers on the extent of irregularities in voter registration
in Ghana in 2008. This research design and analysis explicitly consider the spillovers that may result
from the organization of political parties across multiple electoral areas and the capacity of party agents
to transport supporters from one electoral area to another.
On the other hand, Rundlett and Svolik (2016) wonders why do incumbents who could arguably win
a clean election engage in fraud. This question is motivated because, according to those authors, most
fraud is ultimately executed by a large number of local operatives with an incentive to conceal their
actions. Each agent understands that the difference between her promotion and prosecution rests on
whether her involvement in fraud will result in the incumbent’s eventual victory or defeat. In turn, the
93

===== PAGE 101 =====
agent’s perception of the incumbent’s popularity and the ensuing burden on their collective complicity
plays a crucial coordinating role. The aggregate result is too much fraud for incumbents who need it the
least and too little fraud for those who need it the most. The seeming invincibility of some incumbents
and the surprising fragility of others are thus two sides of the same political logic.
Finally, different papers that we have mentioned and others that we will present later analyze the role
of observers. For example, A. T. Little et al. (2012) states that the good behavior induced by a convincing
election result gives an incentive to commit fraud independent of winning the election by the official tally.
The solid incentive for invite election monitoring to reduce (but not eliminate) expectations about how
much fraud will occur are consistent with incumbents voluntarily inviting monitors and still cheating in
front of them.
8.4 Data
On April 11, 2021, Peru held its general elections where the next president, two vice presidents, 130
congressmen, and 5 Andean parliamentarians were elected for the 2021-2026 government period. In
Peru, there are 27 electoral districts: 24 departments, Metropolitan Lima, the Constitutional Province of
Callao, and residents abroad. The electoral roll consisted of 24.290.921 voters in the national territory
and 997.033 residents abroad, 12.745.409 are women, equivalent to 49,60% and 12.542.545 are men,
equivalent to 50,40%. Metropolitan Lima concentrates 29,89% of voters. The total electoral roll included
25.287.954 qualified voters who exercised their right to vote in 12.846 voting places enabled for this
process.
In Peru, the president and vice-presidents of the Republic are elected by direct suffrage and a sin-
gle electoral district. A second electoral round is held if no presidential candidate reaches more than
50% of the validly cast votes. For the first electoral round, 18 presidential candidacies were presented.
17.713.716 of voters participated, corresponding to 70,048% of the electoral roll, and institutions such as
the Organization of American States (OAS) and the European Union (EU) participated as international
electoral observers. In the results, there were 14.400.630 valid votes (81,296% of votes cast) where the
first two candidates were those of Pedro Castillo, Peru Libre National Political Party, with 2.724.752
(18,921% of valid votes) and Keiko Fujimori, Fuerza Popular, with 1.930.762 (13,407% of valid votes).
Therefore, these two candidates had to participate in the second round for Presidential Election 2021.
On June 6, 2021, the second round for Presidential Election 2021 was held, using the same electoral
roll of the first round. In contrast to the first round, electoral participation in the second round increased
by 4,52 percentage points, reaching 74,568% of the electoral roll. In the results, there were 17.628.497
valid votes (93,486% of votes cast) where Pedro Castillo prevailed with 8.836.380 (50,126% of valid
votes) over Keiko Fujimori with 8.792.117 (49,874% of valid votes). In other words, there were only
44.263 votes of difference, corresponding to 0,175% of the electoral roll and 0,251% of the validly cast
votes.
We collect data from the National Office of Electoral Processes (ONPE). In particular, we use disag-
gregated data at the mesa level, which is the most disaggregated level that exists, for the first (Gobierno
de Datos, 2021a) and second presidential round (Gobierno de Datos, 2021b). The data already includes
94

===== PAGE 102 =====
the modifications, nullity requests, and corrections made by the pertinent institutions.
8.4.1 Share of valid votes
The valid votes consider all the votes that went to any candidate, that is, all the votes that are not null or
blank. Figure 8.4.1 shows that the support for the Peru Libre candidate and the Fuerza Popular candidate
is territorially heterogeneous, with clear majority support for Pedro Castillo in southern Peru and general
in rural areas, sometimes called “Per´u profundo” referring to marginalized areas in terms of political and
economic power and access to some essential services. At the same time, the majority of support for
Keiko Fujimori can be observed in more urban areas, particularly in large cities such as Lima that make
up a third of the electorate.
8.4.2 Voter turnout
As we mentioned in Section 8.3, an important consideration to analyze the integrity of elections is that
the number of votes does not increase or decrease with some irregular pattern. Some previous studies
about electoral fraud analyze the relationship between participation electoral process and the votes for
any of the candidates at the most disaggregated level possible. In this context, in Figure 8.2, we can see
a mesa level graph that shows the percentage of electoral participation on the x-axis and the y-axis the
percentage of votes for the respective party. From this figure, we conclude that we do not observe any
anomaly. An irregularity would be, for example, mesas that have greater electoral participation show
a greater preference for a candidate, or that in mesas where the participation is close to 100% all the
votes go in favor of a single candidate. Those situations would make one suspect that votes favor some
candidates or remove votes from the opponent.
8.5 Participation and voting in first and second round
V oter turnout is not necessarily random. We could not even claim that it is unrelated to fraud. For
example, more observers can make it more difficult for elections to be stolen by reducing the rate of
fraud and increasing the likelihood that fraud will be caught (Hyde, 2007). However, Beaulieu and Hyde
(2009) argue that international benefits for democratic elections give electoral autocrats the incentive to
invite international observers and manipulate elections to minimize international criticism. This increase
in “strategic manipulation” has led to changed incentives for opposition political parties, which have the
most to lose from a manipulated but an internationally certified election. Consequently, international
monitors increase boycott probability, which means greater electoral abstention. On the other hand,
Birch (2010) show that perceptions of electoral integrity are indeed positively associated with propensity
to vote.
Let C denote a vector that summarizes the information related to both geographical characteristics
of the country and electoral organization, for example, a level of aggregation such as regions, electoral
95

===== PAGE 103 =====
districts, polling stations, or mesas. For i “ 1, 2, we define the following binary random variables:
Vi “
" 1, if the voter participated in the i-th round of the election;
0, if not.
It should be remarked that both V1 and V2 are defined on the same finite sample spaceM, namely the set
of voters. This means that the electoral register is the same for both rounds, as in Peru for the first and
second rounds.
8.5.1 Partial identification of the joint distribution of participation
It is impossible to identify the joint distribution of pV1, V2q conditionally on C with the public and
available data since we do not know if a voter has voted on the first and second rounds or only in one
of them. On the other hand, the available data only allows us to identify the corresponding marginal
distributions of V1 and V2, conditionally on C. However, using the Frech ´et inequality (Fr´echet, 1960a),
it is possible to partially identify the joint distribution of pV1, V2q conditionally on C, namely
maxt0, P pV1 “ v1 | Cq ` P pV2 “ v2 | Cq ´ 1u ď P pV1 “ v1, V2 “ v2 | Cq

Let us introduce the following convention: we will say that a partial identification interval is non-
informative when it is equal to r0, 1s. Similarly, we will say that the upper bound of the interval is
non-informative when it is equal to 1; and the lower bound of the interval is non-informative when it is
equal to 0. Therefore, the following comments seem to be relevant:
1. The upper bound of the partial identification interval (8.5.1) is always informative. This is due to
the fact that the proportion of participation in each round is always less than 100%.
2. The lower bound of the partial identification interval (8.5.1) is informative in the following cases:
(a) For pv1, v2q “ p 1, 1q, if P pV1 “ 1 | Cq ą P pV2 “ 0 | Cq, that is, when the participation
rate in the first round is higher than the non-participation rate in the second round.
(b) For pv1, v2q “ p 1, 0q, if P pV1 “ 1 | Cq ą P pV2 “ 1 | Cq, that is, when the participation
rate in the first round is higher than the participation rate in the second round.
(c) For pv1, v2q “ p 0, 1q, if P pV1 “ 0 | Cq ą P pV2 “ 0 | Cq, that is, when the non-participation
rate in the first round is higher than the non-participation rate in the second round.
(d) For pv1, v2q “ p 0, 0q, if P pV1 “ 0 | Cq ą P pV2 “ 1 | Cq, that is, when the non-participation
rate in the first round is higher than the participation rate in the second round.

From these intervals, the following analysis can be made:
1. If P pV2 “ 1 | Cq ă P pV1 “ 1 | Cq, that is, if the participation rate in the second round is lower
than the participation rate in the first round, then the upper bound for P pV2 “ 1 | V1 “ 1, Cq is
informative (different from 1) and the upper bound for P pV1 “ 1 | V2 “ 1, Cq is non-informative
(is exactly one). On the other hand, the informativeness criterion of the lower bound for P pV2 “
1 | V1 “ 1, Cq and P pV1 “ 1 | V2 “ 1, Cq is obtained in a similar way, namely, it is informative
if P pV1 “ 0 | Cq ă P pV2 “ 1 | Cq or, equivalently, P pV1 “ 1 | Cq ą P pV2 “ 0 | Cq. Thus, the
minimum values for P pV2 “ 1 | V1 “ 1, Cq and P pV1 “ 1 | V2 “ 1, Cq are different from zero.
2. If P pV2 “ 1 | Cq ą P pV1 “ 1 | Cq, that is, if the participation rate in the second round is greater
than the participation rate in the first round, then the upper bound for P pV2 “ 1 | V1 “ 1, Cq is
non-informative and the upper bound for P pV1 “ 1 | V2 “ 1, Cq is informative. The lower bound
for P pV2 “ 1 | V1 “ 1, Cq and P pV1 “ 1 | V2 “ 1, Cq is informative if P pV1 “ 0 | Cq ă
P pV2 “ 1 | Cq or, equivalently,P pV1 “ 1 | Cq ą P pV2 “ 0 | Cq.
3. If P pV1 “ 0 | Cq ă P pV2 “ 0 | Cq, that is, if the non-participation rate in the first round is lower
than the non-participation rate in the second round, then the lower bound for P pV2 “ 0 | V1 “
1, Cq is informative and the lower bound for P pV1 “ 0 | V2 “ 1, Cq is non-informative. On the
other hand, the upper bound of P pV2 “ 0 | V1 “ 1, Cq and P pV1 “ 0 | V2 “ 1, Cq is informative
if P pV2 “ 0 | Cq ă P pV1 “ 0 | Cq or, equivalently,P pV2 “ 1 | Cq ą P pV1 “ 1 | Cq.
4. If P pV1 “ 0 | Cq ą P pV2 “ 0 | Cq, that is, if the non-participation rate in the first round is
greater than the non-participation rate in the second round, then the lower bound for P pV2 “ 0 |
V1 “ 1, Cq is non-informative and the lower bound for P pV1 “ 0 | V2 “ 1, Cq is informative.
The upper bound for P pV2 “ 0 | V1 “ 1, Cq and P pV1 “ 0 | V2 “ 1, Cq is informative if
P pV2 “ 0 | Cq ă P pV1 “ 0 | Cq or, equivalently,P pV2 “ 1 | Cq ą P pV1 “ 1 | Cq.
8.5.3 Partial identification of the distributions for each round
Let X be the vector of possible choices in the first round, including blank and null votes. Similarly, letY
be the vector of possible choices in the second round, including blank and invalid votes. Using the Law

of Total Probability, we have that
P pX “ x | C “ cq “ P pX “ x | C “ c, V1 “ 1qP pV1 “ 1 | C “ cq ` P pX “ x | C “ c, V1 “ 0qP pV1 “ 0 | C “ cq.

P pX “ x | C “ c, V1 “ 1qP pV1 “ 1 | C “ cq ď P pX “ x | C “ cq

conditional on C “ c, did not vote. Consequently, the higher this proportion, the higher the bounds for
P pX “ x | C “ cq. Similarly, for the second round, we have
P pY “ y | C “ c, V1 “ 1qP pV1 “ 1 | C “ cq ď P pY “ y | C “ cq

Note that, from in (8.5.2) and (8.5.3), the length of the interval for P pX “ x | C “ cq and P pY “ y |
C “ cq is the proportion of citizens who did not participate in the presidential election.
8.5.4 Empirical analysis of participation and voting using partial identification
Based on the empirical participation percentages that we observe in each round, we can establish intervals
for the probability of voting in the first and second rounds and the probability of voting in the second
round conditional on a vote in the first, as we discussed in Subsection 8.5.2. This probability is of
particular interest since the winner of the elections is defined in the second round.
Table 8.1 shows the analysis of the participation in each round (in each department), including the
probability intervals, described previously. Electoral participation at this level of aggregation shows that
the participation in the second round was more significant than the first one. If we analyze PUNO (a
department in the south of Peru), we can observe that even with high participation in both elections (over
80%), the proportion of people who vote in both rounds is at least 64% and at most 81.91%.
Now, we focus on the probability of voting in the second round, conditional on voting in the first one.
In the last columns, Table 8.1, we find lower and upper bounds. Note that, in all the Departments, the
upper bound is 100%. However, it is important to remark that it is possible to find cases where this does
not occur at a more disaggregated level.
Turning the attention to the lower bound, we found that PUNO presents more than 80% participation
in both rounds. Consequently, the proportion of people who vote in the second round conditional on
voting in the first one is at least one 78.13%. On the other hand, AMAZONAS presents a lower bound
of 38.98%, with participation in both rounds about 60%.

Using the results provided in Subsection 8.5.3, we can provide lower and upper bounds for the proba-

Fuerza Popular in each department. For example, in the second round in TACNA, 25.97% of the citizens
who voted supported Fuerza Popular (the participation here was 79.35%). If all the citizens who did not
vote would vote by another option (Per´u Libre, null or blank), then the proportion of support for Fuerza
Popular would have been 20.61%. On the other hand, if all the citizens who did not vote would vote for
Fuerza Popular, the support would be 41.26%.
Finally, Figure 8.3 shows the bounds described above by department and district. If we analyze the
bounds at the district level, which is the lowest level of granularity in terms of territory, we find an
essential heterogeneity in the electoral support of the Fuerza Popular party. The upper bounds are low at
the south districts of Peru, principally in PUNO, CUZCO, and AY ACUCHO. On the other hand, higher
upper bounds can be found in LORETO districts.
8.6 Electoral fraud
In this section, we propose a simple probability argument for analyzing the fraud, focusing on the second
round results. For simplicity, we assume that fraud occurs when votes are added (or removed) from one
of the candidates. Note that further analysis can be performed assuming that votes were changed from
one candidate to another. Our goal is to determine at what level of fraud the results change.
100

===== PAGE 108 =====
Table 8.2: Analysis of voting in each round
8.6.1 Electoral fraud as a contaminated data problem
Suppose two candidates run in the second ballot of a presidential election. V oters may cast their vote
for one of the candidates, none of them (blank vote), or they may invalidate their vote (null vote). The
winner is the candidate who obtains an absolute majority of the valid votes.
Our framework to analyse the situation is probabilistic. As a matter of fact, the result of a candidate
is the proportion of favorable votes with respect to the number of valid votes; this proportion is what
statistician call arelative frequencyof observations. The probability space,Ωn, contains the identification
number of all n voters with a valid vote. The function y from Ωn into t0, 1u is a random variable taking
value 0 if the voter supports the first candidate and value “1” if she supports the other candidate. In this
setting, candidate “0” wins if and only if
Pnpy “ 0q ą Pnpy “ 1q
where Pn denotes the probability of an event. In this notation the index n reminds that the probability
is computed on discrete events (given that Ωn is discrete). The probability Pn is therefore a statistical
frequency of votes. Note that this framework satisfies all axioms of probability (?, ?) even though Ωn
101

===== PAGE 109 =====
contains the labels of the whole population. Sometimes, in statistics, the probability functionPn is called
the empirical measure.
A fraud is defined as an observed value ypiq that is not equal to the actual vote of the voter i. we do
not consider ballot stuffing! This situation may occur from a variety of reasons (see e.g. XXX for a
discussion) but all with one and only one consequence, being the mismatch between an observed vote
and an actual vote. Let us define a binary random variable, denoted z, indicated if a vote is subject to
fraud or not. The random variable z takes value 1 if there is no mismatch, while the value 0 in the case
of fraud. The observed vote is, therefore, decomposed as follows:
y “ y˚z ` e˚p1 ´ zq
where y˚ denotes the value of the actual vote and e˚ denotes the value of the fraudulent vote. Obviously
when z “ 1 the observed vote equals the actual vote. In case where z “ 0 the observed vote is e˚ whose
value is complementary to y˚.
The above model for the observed vote y is a contaminated model because the pure value of y˚ is
subject to an additive error; for details, see Manski (2007a).
8.6.2 Assessing electoral fraud
Fraudulent votes imply that the proportion of observed votes differ from the proportion of the actual vote.
When the difference is too big, it might imply a different ranking of candidates. This is the situation we
address in our study. An electoral fraud has an impact on the ranking if either
Pnpy “ 1q ą Pnpy “ 0q and Pnpy˚ “ 1q ă Pnpy˚ “ 0q
or
Pnpy “ 1q ă Pnpy “ 0q and Pnpy˚ “ 1q ą Pnpy˚ “ 0q .
These definitions are not operational because we cannot compute Pnpy˚ “ tq, with t P t 0, 1u, from
data. However, we can raise the following question : what is the minimal fraud rate such that fraud has
an impact on the ranking? We now provide a formal answer to this question.
Denote the fraud rate by p “ Pnpz “ 1q. From the law of total probability we may write
Pnpy “ 1q “ Pnpy “ 1|z “ 0qp1 ´ pq ` Pnpy “ 1|z “ 1qp
According to our model for y, when there is no fraud we can replace Pnpy “ 1|z “ 0q by Pnpy˚ “
1|z “ 0q. Similarly when there is fraud then Pnpy “ 1|z “ 1q equals Pnpy˚ “ 0|z “ 1q “ 1 ´ Pnpy˚ “
1|z “ 1q. Finally, we get
Pnpy “ 1q “ Pnpy˚ “ 1|z “ 0qp1 ´ pq ` t1 ´ Pnpy˚ “ 1|z “ 1qup . (8.6.4)
Moreover, the law of total probability also implies
Pnpy˚ “ 1q “ Pnpy˚ “ 1|z “ 0qp1 ´ pq ` Pnpy˚ “ 1|z “ 1qp .

8.7 More than two candidates
In an election with more that two candidates, the question that can be raised is the following: what is the
minimal fraud rate that preserves the rank between the candidates? The previous result for 2 candidates
can be generalized, although we need an argument that does not rely on the binarity of the choice.
The observed vote is now a random variable y taking values in t1, 2, . . . , Ju if we consider J candi-

y “ y˚z ` e˚p1 ´ zq

The use of our result must depend on the political analysis of interest. In a two-rounds election, the

first. In that case J “ 3 and the level of confidence requires a fraud rate lower than 0.0082 (that is
0.0166/2). Considering all the 18 candidates in a two round election is probably useless since there are
very small differences at the bottom of the table, and those little difference will drive the minimum value
in inequality (8.7.8).
8.8 Discussion
In this study we find firstly that electoral participation entails significant uncertainty when it is low and
that therefore we must be careful when it comes to understanding and analyzing electoral support when
we are under this scenario, secondly than when constructing a model of electoral fraud with parameters
that incorporate this fraud both in its quantity and in its direction we can understand how the results of an
election vary against it, thirdly under this same fraud model we can formulate a series of hypotheses that
account for of the impact that the cancellation of polling stations and their subsequent reinsertion can
have, correcting fraudulent votes instead of canceling them, and in fourth, we could observe throughout
this study that in a vote as narrow as that of Peru, electoral fraud, and in general, the analysis of electoral
results, distortions in the electoral process and their respective complaints has n to be taken seriously and
with the weight that corresponds because they compromise electoral integrity.
The practical contribution of this study lies in the simulation of the allegations of electoral fraud, es-
pecially in one of the most widespread such as the alleged impossibility that the defeated candidate has
received 0 votes in some polls, which we show that even if it were true, It did not change who was pro-
claimed president of Peru. On the other hand, regarding the theoretical contribution, this study proposes
a different perspective regarding the proposed methodologies and we believe that the parameterization of
electoral fraud, although simple as in the models we observe, can provide us with a valuable tool to study
the impact Regarding the electoral results that the different forms of fraud exposed may have, however,
105

===== PAGE 113 =====
we propose in what remains of this section possible extensions of this model that may be of particular
interest to different sectors of society.
The participation and fraud tools could interact, but for this it would be quite attractive to assume
certain assumptions from the political sciences for the behavior of the voters, for example, that the one
who voted in the first round for an ideologically right-wing candidate votes in the second round by the
candidate who is on the ballot that is closest to that ideology, or even that assumption could be relaxed
and simply assume that whoever voted for one of the candidates who went to the second round and voted
in both rounds, vote in the second round for that same candidate. Where, as we saw in sectors with high
participation in both rounds, the probability of voting in the second given that they voted in the first is
also high even if we partially achieve that identification.
However, the techniques presented here are analytical tools that can be extensible and flexible, where
we could include in the model’s parameterization different types of fraud, such as differentiating between
changing the vote of one candidate to another, or changing the vote of one candidate. candidate to null,
or change a vote from null to a candidate. In any case, in this work we show a first step for the analysis
of the electoral process where we warn the sensitivity of the results to electoral participation, as well as
we propose a model that allows proposing multiple hypotheses regarding the size and direction of the
electoral fraud to conclude if they are hypotheses or accusations regarding fraud would have modified
who is proclaimed the winner in the elections.
For cases in which the analysis focuses on suspicions of fraud that are originated by the characteristics
of the polling place, a C could be defined that comes from the tradition of point estimation or econo-
metrics where the vector includes characteristics as if the V oting place belongs to rural areas, if it has
telephone coverage, public investment in the sector, poverty rates, etc.
This model can also be extended to the analysis of the organizations and institutions in charge of the
electoral process, where they can observe the impact of certain decisions, irregularities and nullity of the
acts within the same day of the elections as with subsequent requests in around this process.
A future development of this model may be to model the value ofp through intervals as in the Horowitz
and Manski study J. L. Horowitz and Manski (1995), where although they do it to find ”Contaminated
and Corrupted Data” in a database of households, we in this study could do the same, considering the
”not interviewed” as those who did not participate in the electoral process while on the electoral roll, and
from some assumptions from the political sciences it could be possible to determine these bound ofp. In
addition, the aforementioned work is strongly recommended if you want to deepen regarding the partial
identification and parameterization of errors from its algebraic and statistical development.

Chapter 9
A Critical View on the NEAT Equating
Design: Statistical Modelling and
Identifiability Problems
9.1 Introduction
Test equating1 is conducted to adjust the score scales of different test forms in order to compensate for
differences in relative difficulty and thus make scores equivalent and comparable (Lord, 1950; Angoff,
1984; Kolen & Brennan, 2014). The equating problem consists in mapping scores defined on one scale
into their equivalents on the other scale. Such mapping is achieved using what is called an equating
transformation function (for details, see Gonz´alez & Wiberg, 2017, Chapter 1). Although different types
of score linkages are distinguished in the literature (?, ?), with equating being a particular case of score
linking, the same linking function can be used in each case (Kolen, 2007) leading to scores that are
interpreted as either interchangeable (equating) or comparable (linking).
Because score differences can also arise due to ability differences of test takers, comparable groups of
examinees must be used when collecting score data to estimate the equating function. Different strategies
for collecting score data have been proposed in the literature, leading to what are called equating designs;
see, e.g., von Davier, Holland, and Thayer (2004), Kolen and Brennan (2014) and Gonz´alez and Wiberg
(2017). These designs differ in that either common persons or common items are used to perform the
score transformation, and in the conditions and assumptions made to collect the score data. In this chapter
we will focus the attention on the nonequivalent groups with anchor test design (NEAT).
The NEAT design (a.k.a the common item nonequivalent group design, CINEG) is widely used in
test equating. Under this design, two groups of test takers are administered different test forms that
are intended to measure a common variable, with each test form containing a subset of common items.
1This chapter reproduces the chapter entitled A Critical View on the NEAT Equating Design: Statistical Modelling and
Identifiability Problems by E. San Mart´ın and J. Gonz´alez, which was accepted for publication in the Journal of Educational
and Behavioral Statistics.
110

===== PAGE 118 =====
Because test takers from different groups respond to only one test form, missing score data emerge by
design rendering some of the score distributions unavailable. The partially observed score data formally
leads to an identifiability problem, which has not been recognized as such in the equating literature, and
has been considered from different perspectives, all of them making different assumptions in order to
estimate the unidentified score distributions; see,e.g., Sinharay and Holland (2010), Miyazaki, Hoshino,
Mayekawa, and Shigemasu (2009) and Holland, Sinharay, von Davier, and Han (2008).
In this chapter, we specify the statistical model underlying the NEAT design using a fully probabilistic
approach. By so doing, we not only show that the notion of synthetic population is meaningless, but
also offer an alternative formal and useful conceptualization of it. One of the consequences of this
conceptualization is that the population weights used in the definition of a synthetic population cannot
be arbitrarily chosen. Moreover, using a partial identification approach (Manski, 2007a; Tamer, 2010a),
we make explicit the devastating results implied by the identification problem that are hidden when the
missing at random condition is assumed.
The chapter is organized as follows: in Section 9.2 current definitions and assumptions considered
when conducting equating under the NEAT design are revised. Next, we criticize aspects of the current
view on NEAT equating, make explicit the statement of the problem to be discussed, and explain the
strategy used in the chapter. In Section 9.3 the statistical model underlying the NEAT design is defined
and the identifiability problem is described. In Section 9.4 we describe two approaches to tackle the
identifiability problem and study the impact of the lack of identifiability in the actual equating using the
theory of quantiles. An empirical illustration of the main findings is also presented at the end of this
section. In the context of score linking, in Section 9.5 we discuss alternative identification restrictions
leading to improve the previous results. The chapter finalizes in Section 9.6 summarizing the main points
and discussing on how severe the identifiability problem can be in the context of test equating.
9.2 Modelling problems underlying the NEAT design
In order to show what motivates our critique of the NEAT design, we first review the basic definitions
and strategies given in the literature for estimating the corresponding equating transformation.
9.2.1 Equating under the NEAT design: a summary of the literature
Let X and Y be random variables representing the scores on tests forms X and Y , respectively. The
equating problem is addressed by using an equating function φ : X Ý ÑY, where X and Y are the
sets of all possible scores on test forms X and Y (the score scales), so that for each raw score x we can
compute the corresponding y “ φpxq (Braun & Holland, 1982; Gonz ´alez & Wiberg, 2017). Following
Lord (1950) and Angoff (1984), a function φ can be defined taking into account that “two scores, one on
Form X and the other on Form Y [ . . .] may be considered equivalent if their corresponding percentile
ranks in any given group are equal” (Angoff, 1984, p.86). More specifically, the function φ : X Ý ÑY
is defined as follows: for each x P X , we associate y .“ φpxq P Y such that
FY rφpxqs “ FX pxq; (9.2.1)
111

===== PAGE 119 =====
the symbol .“ means “defined as”. Although equation (9.2.1) can always be solved using generalized
inverses (Embrechts & Hofert, 2013), meaningful equating results are obtained only when scores are
continuous (van der Linden, 2019). This is why all practical applications of test equating make use of
what is called a continuization step; for details, see Braun and Holland (1982), Kolen and Brennan (2014)
and Gonz´alez and Wiberg (2017).
Consider now a third random variable, A, which represent the scores obtained on an anchor test form
A. Two definitions of the NEAT design appearing in the standard literature on equating are the following:
In the Non-Equivalent groups with Anchor Test (NEAT) Design there are two populations,P and Q,
of test-takers and a sample of examinees from each. The sample from P takes test X, the sample
from Q takes test Y , and both samples take a set of common items, the anchor test A (von Davier et
al., 2004, p.32).
For [the NEAT] design, two groups of examinees from different populations are each administered
different test forms that have a set of items in common (Kolen & Brennan, 2014, p.103).
According to Braun and Holland (1982), the function φ should be defined with respect to a single
population. This is a challenging task when performing equating under the NEAT design as, in fact, two
populations, P and Q, are involved. Consequently, P and Q “must be combined to obtain a single pop-
ulation for defining [the] equating function” (Kolen & Brennan, 2014, p.103), which leads to introduce
a synthetic population, also called target population, denoted by T . This is done by “conceptualizing
a larger population that has P and Q as two mutually and exhaustive strata” (Braun & Holland, 1982,
p.21), a “combined group, some of whom would be taking Form X, other taking Form Y , and all taking
Form A, the anchor test” (?, ?, p.296), “a mixture of bothP and Q” (von Davier et al., 2004, p.34). Each
of these statements is assumed to be represented by the following expression:
T “ ω P ` p1 ´ ωq Q, (9.2.2)
where the populations P and Q are weighted by ω P r0, 1s and 1 ´ ω, respectively. However, P and Q
are just labels denoting the two populations and thus the expression (9.2.2) would represent a “weighted
average of labels” which, of course, does not make any sense. A consequence of using this expression is
that the target score distributions are arbitrarily chosen, as it will be seen in Section 3.
The equating transformation is then typically computed after defining the score distributions of X, Y
and A on the synthetic population T . Thus, for instance, for a specific and arbitrary choice of ω, the
distribution FXT pxq, which has also been denoted in the literature as P pX ď x | T q (von Davier et al.,
2004, p.7) (Sinharay & Holland, 2010), is obtained by first computing the distribution of raw scores X
for each strata P and Q, and then forming weighted averages of these distributions using ω and 1 ´ ω,
namely
FXT pxq .“ ω FXP pxq ` p1 ´ ωq FXQpxq. (9.2.3)
The score distributions FXP pxq and FXQpxq have been denoted in the literature as P pX ď x | Pq
and P pX ď x | Qq, respectively, with similar notation used for FY T pyq and FAT paq. From the data
collected under the NEAT design, the distributions FXP pxq, FY Qpyq, FAP paq and FAQpaq may be
112

===== PAGE 120 =====
computed. Nevertheless, the distributions FXQpxq and FY P pyq are not directly estimable. They are
typically obtained by conditioning on the anchor score A and using the following assumptions:
(i) FXP|Apx | aq “ FXQ|Apx | aq; (ii) FY P|Apy | aq “ FY Q|Apy | aq, (9.2.4)
where FXP|A is the distribution function of X conditionally on A in P; and the other distributions have
a similar meaning. Under these assumptions, the target score distributions are given by
(i) FXT pxq “ ω FXP pxq ` p1 ´ ωq
ÿ

FXP|Apx | aq P pA “ a | Qq;
(ii) FY T pyq “ ω

where A is the set of possible anchor-test scores. These score distributions are then used to computeφpxq
for a given x P X using the equality φpxq “ F ´1

equating (FEE, Angoff, 1984). Other methods used for equating under the NEAT design such as the
chained equipercentile equating (CEE) and IRT-Observed Score equating (IRT-OSE), together with the
assumptions needed in each case to obtain the distributions that are not directly estimable are described
in Sinharay and Holland (2010).
9.2.2 What we question: A statement of the problem
From a modelling point of view, equating under the NEAT design depends on both the synthetic popula-
tion (9.2.2) and the assumptions in (9.2.4), which are typically known as themissing-at-random assump-
tions (Liou & Cheng, 1995; Miyazaki et al., 2009; Sinharay & Holland, 2010). Bearing in mind that the
equating function is defined in probabilistic terms, the following questions arise:
1. Taking into account that P and Q are not random variables but mere labels, under which conditions
are P pX ď x | Pq and P pX ď x | Qq properly understood as conditional probability functions?
2. If such conditions exist, would it be possible to define the concept of a synthetic population in
probabilistic terms?
3. Is it possible to provide alternative assumptions to the ones given in (9.2.4) to solve the problem
of missing score data underlying the NEAT design? If so, what would be the impact of such
alternative assumptions on the NEAT design in specific contexts?
The answers to the previous questions depend on the answer to a more fundamental one: how can “an
understanding of the way in which the data” collected under the NEAT design “are supposed to, or did
in fact, originate” be obtained (Fisher, 1973, p.8)? This question can be answered once the parameters
of interest that “describe [the observations] exhaustively in respect of all qualities under discussion” (?,
?, p.311) are made explicit. Nevertheless, the data generating process is not necessarily characterized by
the parameters of interest. When this is the case, we face an identifiability problem. This is the problem
we will unveil in the NEAT design.
113

===== PAGE 121 =====
9.3 Statistical specification of the NEAT design and the inherent identifi-
cation problem
One way to answer these questions is to follow Gonz´alez and Wiberg (2017)’s perspective, which consists
in specifying the NEAT design as a statistical model. This means to make explicit the following three
components:
1. The sample space pM, Mq, where M contains the elementary events and M is a class containing
the events of interest and their combinations through unions of sets as well as complement of a set
–this is why M is a σ-field of subsets of M. The pair pM, Mq is technically called a measurable
space.
2. The sampling probabilities P γ, indexed by a parameter γ, where each P γ is defined on pM, Mq.
3. The parameter space Γ, which represents the set of all plausible values of γ;
for details, see San Mart´ın et al. (2015) and references therein.
What are the practical advantages of this formalism? First, the population of interest is made explicit
by listing it through the elements of the sample space. Second, features of the population under study
are fully characterized by the parameters indexing the sampling probabilities. Third, this formalism
allows us to evaluate whether certain characteristics relevant to researchers can indeed be represented as
(a function of) parameters: when this is not the case, then we face an identification problem (?, ?).
The specification of the statistical model underlying the NEAT design will be performed in a sequential
way, which leads to decompose the joint probability distribution defined on all the pertinent random
variables into specific conditional sub-models. The sequential specification makes explicit the way in
which the data are (supposed to be) generated (Wunsch, Mouchart, & Russo, 2014). This sequential
specification is critically based on the Law of Total Probability in the sense that it ensures the existence
of the conditional distribution of a new random variable given the previously introduced variables. This
corresponds to the formal definition of a conditional probability function as introduced by ? (?, §6).
By doing so, it will be made explicit which are the parameters of the statistical model and the lack of
identifiability of the parameters of interest.
9.3.1 The sample space in the NEAT design
In order to construct both the random variables and the probability distributions underlying the NEAT
design, it is necessary to make explicit the sample space M. In the case of the NEAT design, M corre-
sponds to the union of the index sets labeling the two mutually exclusive groups of examinees who were
exposed to the test forms X and Y , namely
M “ P Y Q, where P “ ti1, . . . , inP u, Q “ tj1, . . . , jnQu, P X Q “ H,
nP and nQ are the total number of examinees exposed to test forms X and Y , respectively. Thus, M
corresponds to the population of interest underlying the NEAT design. Since it is a finite set, the class
M of events of interest corresponds to the class of subsets of M.

9.3.2 The random variable Z
All the random variables underlying the NEAT design should be defined from M to a set of possible
values. In addition to the variables representing the test scores, namely X, Y, A, we construct a random
variable representing the groups underlying the NEAT design. To do so, remind that, by definition,
given two measurable spaces pM, Mq and pN, N q, a function f : M Ý ÑN is a random variable if
f ´1pBq P M for all B P N (?, ?, ?). In the finite discrete case, as it is in the NEAT design, this
formal definition reduces to the following characterization: a mapping of the set M into a set of values
is a random variable if and only if the mapping induces a partition of M. For instance, for the case
when P “ t i1, i2, i3u and Q “ t j1, j2, j3, j4, j5u, consider the toy data set shown in Table 9.1. If
A : M Ý ÑA .“ t 0, 1, 2, 3u is a random variable representing the scores obtained in an anchor test of
three items, then A´1t0u “ t j4u, A´1t1u “ t i2, j3u, A´1t2u “ t i1, i3, j5u and A´1t3u “ t j1, j2u.
These sets belong to M and constitute a partition of M.
This characterization is relevant for at least two reasons: first, different examinees with equal values on
a random variable will be treated as equivalent and put in the same equivalence class (i.e., the members
of the partition) of examinees. Second, a random variable is the class of events in M (in the finite case,
subsets of M) that is induced by the possible values the random variable can take (?, ?), which means
that all information provided by a random variable is captured in the sample space pM, Mq.
The sample spaceM has been defined through the partitiontP, Qu, which implies that a binary random
variable Z can naturally be defined as
Z “
" 1, if the examenee belongs to group P;
0, if the examenee belongs to group Q. (9.3.1)
That is, the preimage Z´1t1u corresponds to the event P, and the preimage Z´1t0u, to the event Q: this
is the semantic meaning of Z.
A natural first approach to construct a probability function of Z would be thinking in terms of exper-
imental units (the members of the sample space M) and a random mechanism that assigns numbers to
the experimental units, as for instance scores or measurements (Lord & Novick, 1968, Sections 1.3 and
115

===== PAGE 123 =====
2.2). Under this perspective, the groups P and Q could be viewed as “realizations” or “occurrences” of
such a mechanism. However, using R. J. Little and Rubin (1994)’s terminology, the groups P and Q in
the NEAT design are considered as selected samples and, consequently, subject to selection bias. Thus,
this approach is not useful to reveal some random assignment mechanism that makes sense of Z in the
NEAT design.
Nevertheless, it is possible to propose an alternative approach that suits better the characteristics of the
NEAT design. To do so, we resort to Carnap (1962, §12)’s distinction between what a researcher says
about a term in his formulation (semantics) and what he actually does with that term in the corpus of his
formulation (syntax): the sense of a certain term is revealed from its use in formal arguments. Taking
into account that, from an axiomatic perspective, “random” and “realization” or “occurrence” are mere
terms, not probabilistic concepts (see ? (?, p.2) and It ˆo (1984, pp.1,3), respectively), an axiomatically
well defined probability function of Z can be defined as follows: when M “ t m1, . . . , mku is a finite
set, it is enough to take an arbitrary set of non-negative numbers tp1, . . . , pku with their sum equal to 1
such that P tmiu “ pi for i “ 1, . . . , k(?, ?, p.3). Consequently, a possible probability function ofZ can
accordingly be constructed by considering the relative sizes of the groups P and Q (Braun & Holland,
1982), namely
P pZ “ 1q “ nP
nP ` nQ
, P pZ “ 0q “ nQ
nP ` nQ
. (9.3.2)
Under this approach, both P and Q are viewed as constituting the population of interest, rather than
realizations from some sampling mechanism (see, Manski, 2013b, p.126). This is due to the fact that
we focus our attention on the identification problem underlying the NEAT design, which will be the
same regardless of the type of sampling process it is reasonable to assume; see also Manski and Nagin
(1998, specially p.109). It is in fact possible to specify a different probability function for Z, but its
identifiability should be justified, as it is the case for the one defined in (9.3.2). Furthermore, in the
identification analysis that will follow, the role played by the random variable Z is only through the
probability function that is defined on it.
As for its semantic meaning, the partition of M induced by Z can be interpreted as a “sample by
specification” (Lord & Novick, 1968, Section 2.5), thus avoiding the idea of a possible random as-
signment mechanism. In any case, we define a “random assignment mechanism” in terms of “absence
of bias”, which in turn is rigorously described in terms of conditional probabilities; for details, see
Appendix 9.7. This definition is actually motivated by a semantic relationship between both notions
(Stephenson & Imrie, 1998; Odgaard-Jensen et al., 2011; Bhide, Shah, & Acharya, 2018). To the best of
our knowledge, no formal proof exists deriving “absence of bias” from “random assignment” precisely
because this last notion is not a probabilistic concept. As it will be seen in what follows, the identifi-
cation problem arises from the selection bias inherent to the NEAT design, which formally means that
P pX ď x | A, Z “ 1q ‰ P pX ď x | A, Z “ 0q and P pY ď y | A, Z “ 1q ‰ P pY ď y | A, Z “ 0q.
Before discussing this aspect, let us follow through the sequential specification of the NEAT design.
